And\xe2\x80\x94and\xe2\x80\x94what a fine day it is, isn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nit, Hannah, for this time of year? Be you going to market next\r\nSaturday? How is your aunt now?\xe2\x80\x9d And so on, says Tony, to keep her from\r\ntalking any more about love in Milly\xe2\x80\x99s hearing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But he found his work cut out for him, and wondering again how he\r\nshould get out of this ticklish business, he looked about for a chance.\r\nNearing home he saw his father in a field not far off, holding up his\r\nhand as if he wished to speak to Tony.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cWould you mind taking the reins a moment, Hannah,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, much\r\nrelieved, \xe2\x80\x9cwhile I go and find out what father wants?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98She consented, and away he hastened into the field, only too glad to\r\nget breathing time. He found that his father was looking at him with\r\nrather a stern eye.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cCome, come, Tony,\xe2\x80\x9d says old Mr. Kytes, as soon as his son was\r\nalongside him, \xe2\x80\x9cthis won\xe2\x80\x99t do, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d says Tony.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, if you mean to marry Milly Richards, do it, and there\xe2\x80\x99s an end\r\no\xe2\x80\x99t. But don\xe2\x80\x99t go driving about the country with Jolliver\xe2\x80\x99s daughter\r\nand making a scandal. I won\xe2\x80\x99t have such things done.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cI only asked her\xe2\x80\x94that is, she asked me, to ride home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cShe? Why, now, if it had been Milly, \xe2\x80\x99twould have been quite proper;\r\nbut you and Hannah Jolliver going about by yourselves\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cMilly\xe2\x80\x99s there too, father.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cMilly? Where?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cUnder the corn-sacks! Yes, the truth is, father, I\xe2\x80\x99ve got rather into\r\na nunny-watch, I\xe2\x80\x99m afeard! Unity Sallet is there too\xe2\x80\x94yes, at the other\r\nend, under the tarpaulin. All three are in that waggon, and what to do\r\nwith \xe2\x80\x99em I know no more than the dead! The best plan is, as I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nthinking, to speak out loud and plain to one of \xe2\x80\x99em before the rest,\r\nand that will settle it; not but what \xe2\x80\x99twill cause \xe2\x80\x99em to kick up a bit\r\nof a miff, for certain. Now which would you marry, father, if you was\r\nin my place?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cWhichever of \xe2\x80\x99em did _not_ ask to ride with thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cThat was Milly, I\xe2\x80\x99m bound to say, as she only mounted by my\r\ninvitation. But Milly\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen stick to Milly, she\xe2\x80\x99s the best . . . But look at that!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98His father pointed toward the waggon. \xe2\x80\x9cShe can\xe2\x80\x99t hold that horse in.\r\nYou shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have left the reins in her hands. Run on and take the\r\nhorse\xe2\x80\x99s head, or there\xe2\x80\x99ll be some accident to them maids!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Tony\xe2\x80\x99s horse, in fact, in spite of Hannah\xe2\x80\x99s tugging at the reins, had\r\nstarted on his way at a brisk walking pace, being very anxious to get\r\nback to the stable, for he had had a long day out. Without another word\r\nTony rushed away from his father to overtake the horse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Now of all things that could have happened to wean him from Milly\r\nthere was nothing so powerful as his father\xe2\x80\x99s recommending her. No; it\r\ncould not be Milly, after all. Hannah must be the one, since he could\r\nnot marry all three. This he thought while running after the waggon.\r\nBut queer things were happening inside it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It was, of course, Milly who had screamed under the sack-bags, being\r\nobliged to let off her bitter rage and shame in that way at what Tony\r\nwas saying, and never daring to show, for very pride and dread o\xe2\x80\x99 being\r\nlaughed at, that she was in hiding. She became more and more restless,\r\nand in twisting herself about, what did she see but another woman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfoot and white stocking close to her head. It quite frightened her, not\r\nknowing that Unity Sallet was in the waggon likewise. But after the\r\nfright was over she determined to get to the bottom of all this, and\r\nshe crept and crept along the bed of the waggon, under the tarpaulin,\r\nlike a snake, when lo and behold she came face to face with Unity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cWell, if this isn\xe2\x80\x99t disgraceful!\xe2\x80\x9d says Milly in a raging whisper to\r\nUnity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis,\xe2\x80\x9d says Unity, \xe2\x80\x9cto see you hiding in a young man\xe2\x80\x99s waggon like\r\nthis, and no great character belonging to either of ye!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cMind what you are saying!\xe2\x80\x9d replied Milly, getting louder. \xe2\x80\x9cI am\r\nengaged to be married to him, and haven\xe2\x80\x99t I a right to be here? What\r\nright have you, I should like to know? What has he been promising you?\r\nA pretty lot of nonsense, I expect! But what Tony says to other women\r\nis all mere wind, and no concern to me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you be too sure!\xe2\x80\x9d says Unity. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s going to have Hannah, and\r\nnot you, nor me either; I could hear that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Now at these strange voices sounding from under the cloth Hannah was\r\nthunderstruck a\xe2\x80\x99most into a swound; and it was just at this time that\r\nthe horse moved on. Hannah tugged away wildly, not knowing what she was\r\ndoing; and as the quarrel rose louder and louder Hannah got so\r\nhorrified that she let go the reins altogether. The horse went on at\r\nhis own pace, and coming to the corner where we turn round to drop down\r\nthe hill to Lower Longpuddle he turned too quick, the off wheels went\r\nup the bank, the waggon rose sideways till it was quite on edge upon\r\nthe near axles, and out rolled the three maidens into the road in a\r\nheap.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98When Tony came up, frightened and breathless, he was relieved enough\r\nto see that neither of his darlings was hurt, beyond a few scratches\r\nfrom the brambles of the hedge. But he was rather alarmed when he heard\r\nhow they were going on at one another.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t ye quarrel, my dears\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t ye!\xe2\x80\x9d says he, taking off his hat out\r\nof respect to \xe2\x80\x99em. And then he would have kissed them all round, as\r\nfair and square as a man could, but they were in too much of a taking\r\nto let him, and screeched and sobbed till they was quite spent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cNow I\xe2\x80\x99ll speak out honest, because I ought to,\xe2\x80\x9d says Tony, as soon as\r\nhe could get heard. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd this is the truth,\xe2\x80\x9d says he. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve asked\r\nHannah to be mine, and she is willing, and we are going to put up the\r\nbanns next\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Tony had not noticed that Hannah\xe2\x80\x99s father was coming up behind, nor\r\nhad he noticed that Hannah\xe2\x80\x99s face was beginning to bleed from the\r\nscratch of a bramble. Hannah had seen her father, and had run to him,\r\ncrying worse than ever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cMy daughter is _not_ willing, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d says Mr. Jolliver hot and strong.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe you willing, Hannah? I ask ye to have spirit enough to refuse him,\r\nif yer virtue is left to \xe2\x80\x99ee and you run no risk?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s as sound as a bell for me, that I\xe2\x80\x99ll swear!\xe2\x80\x9d says Tony, flaring\r\nup. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd so\xe2\x80\x99s the others, come to that, though you may think it an\r\nonusual thing in me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cI have spirit, and I do refuse him!\xe2\x80\x9d says Hannah, partly because her\r\nfather was there, and partly, too, in a tantrum because of the\r\ndiscovery, and the scratch on her face. \xe2\x80\x9cLittle did I think when I was\r\nso soft with him just now that I was talking to such a false deceiver!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cWhat, you won\xe2\x80\x99t have me, Hannah?\xe2\x80\x9d says Tony, his jaw hanging down\r\nlike a dead man\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cNever\xe2\x80\x94I would sooner marry no\xe2\x80\x94nobody at all!\xe2\x80\x9d she gasped out, though\r\nwith her heart in her throat, for she would not have refused Tony if he\r\nhad asked her quietly, and her father had not been there, and her face\r\nhad not been scratched by the bramble. And having said that, away she\r\nwalked upon her father\xe2\x80\x99s arm, thinking and hoping he would ask her\r\nagain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Tony didn\xe2\x80\x99t know what to say next. Milly was sobbing her heart out;\r\nbut as his father had strongly recommended her he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t feel\r\ninclined that way. So he turned to Unity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cWell, will you, Unity dear, be mine?\xe2\x80\x9d he says.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cTake her leavings? Not I!\xe2\x80\x9d says Unity. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d scorn it!\xe2\x80\x9d And away walks\r\nUnity Sallet likewise, though she looked back when she\xe2\x80\x99d gone some way,\r\nto see if he was following her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98So there at last were left Milly and Tony by themselves, she crying in\r\nwatery streams, and Tony looking like a tree struck by lightning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Milly,\xe2\x80\x9d he says at last, going up to her, \xe2\x80\x9cit do seem as if\r\nfate had ordained that it should be you and I, or nobody. And what must\r\nbe must be, I suppose. Hey, Milly?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cIf you like, Tony. You didn\xe2\x80\x99t really mean what you said to them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cNot a word of it!\xe2\x80\x9d declares Tony, bringing down his fist upon his\r\npalm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And then he kissed her, and put the waggon to rights, and they mounted\r\ntogether; and their banns were put up the very next Sunday. I was not\r\nable to go to their wedding, but it was a rare party they had, by all\r\naccount. Everybody in Longpuddle was there almost; you among the rest,\r\nI think, Mr. Flaxton?\xe2\x80\x99 The speaker turned to the parish clerk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I was,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Flaxton. \xe2\x80\x98And that party was the cause of a very\r\ncurious change in some other people\xe2\x80\x99s affairs; I mean in Steve\r\nHardcome\xe2\x80\x99s and his cousin James\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ah! the Hardcomes,\xe2\x80\x99 said the stranger. \xe2\x80\x98How familiar that name is to\r\nme! What of them?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe clerk cleared his throat and began:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE HISTORY OF THE HARDCOMES\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, Tony\xe2\x80\x99s was the very best wedding-randy that ever I was at; and\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve been at a good many, as you may suppose\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94turning to the\r\nnewly-arrived one\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98having as a church-officer, the privilege to attend\r\nall christening, wedding, and funeral parties\xe2\x80\x94such being our Wessex\r\ncustom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x99Twas on a frosty night in Christmas week, and among the folk invited\r\nwere the said Hardcomes o\xe2\x80\x99 Climmerston\xe2\x80\x94Steve and James\xe2\x80\x94first cousins,\r\nboth of them small farmers, just entering into business on their own\r\naccount. With them came, as a matter of course, their intended wives,\r\ntwo young women of the neighbourhood, both very pretty and sprightly\r\nmaidens, and numbers of friends from Abbot\xe2\x80\x99s-Cernel, and Weatherbury,\r\nand Mellstock, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t know where\xe2\x80\x94a regular houseful.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The kitchen was cleared of furniture for dancing, and the old folk\r\nplayed at \xe2\x80\x9cPut\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cAll-fours\xe2\x80\x9d in the parlour, though at last they\r\ngave that up to join in the dance. The top of the figure was by the\r\nlarge front window of the room, and there were so many couples that the\r\nlower part of the figure reached through the door at the back, and into\r\nthe darkness of the out-house; in fact, you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t see the end of the\r\nrow at all, and \xe2\x80\x99twas never known exactly how long that dance was, the\r\nlowest couples being lost among the faggots and brushwood in the\r\nout-house.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98When we had danced a few hours, and the crowns of we taller men were\r\nswelling into lumps with bumping the beams of the ceiling, the first\r\nfiddler laid down his fiddle-bow, and said he should play no more, for\r\nhe wished to dance. And in another hour the second fiddler laid down\r\nhis, and said he wanted to dance too; so there was only the third\r\nfiddler left, and he was a\xe2\x80\x99 old, veteran man, very weak in the wrist.\r\nHowever, he managed to keep up a faltering tweedle-dee; but there being\r\nno chair in the room, and his knees being as weak as his wrists, he was\r\nobliged to sit upon as much of the little corner-table as projected\r\nbeyond the corner-cupboard fixed over it, which was not a very wide\r\nseat for a man advanced in years.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Among those who danced most continually were the two engaged couples,\r\nas was natural to their situation. Each pair was very well matched, and\r\nvery unlike the other. James Hardcome\xe2\x80\x99s intended was called Emily\r\nDarth, and both she and James were gentle, nice-minded, in-door people,\r\nfond of a quiet life. Steve and his chosen, named Olive Pawle, were\r\ndifferent; they were of a more bustling nature, fond of racketing about\r\nand seeing what was going on in the world. The two couples had arranged\r\nto get married on the same day, and that not long thence; Tony\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwedding being a sort of stimulant, as is often the case; I\xe2\x80\x99ve noticed\r\nit professionally many times.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98They danced with such a will as only young people in that stage of\r\ncourtship can dance; and it happened that as the evening wore on James\r\nhad for his partner Stephen\xe2\x80\x99s plighted one, Olive, at the same time\r\nthat Stephen was dancing with James\xe2\x80\x99s Emily. It was noticed that in\r\nspite o\xe2\x80\x99 the exchange the young men seemed to enjoy the dance no less\r\nthan before. By and by they were treading another tune in the same\r\nchanged order as we had noticed earlier, and though at first each one\r\nhad held the other\xe2\x80\x99s mistress strictly at half-arm\xe2\x80\x99s length, lest there\r\nshould be shown any objection to too close quarters by the lady\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nproper man, as time passed there was a little more closeness between\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em; and presently a little more closeness still.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The later it got the more did each of the two cousins dance with the\r\nwrong young girl, and the tighter did he hold her to his side as he\r\nwhirled her round; and, what was very remarkable, neither seemed to\r\nmind what the other was doing. The party began to draw towards its end,\r\nand I saw no more that night, being one of the first to leave, on\r\naccount of my morning\xe2\x80\x99s business. But I learnt the rest of it from\r\nthose that knew.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98After finishing a particularly warming dance with the changed\r\npartners, as I\xe2\x80\x99ve mentioned, the two young men looked at one another,\r\nand in a moment or two went out into the porch together.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cJames,\xe2\x80\x9d says Steve, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat were you thinking of when you were dancing\r\nwith my Olive?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said James, \xe2\x80\x9cperhaps what you were thinking of when you were\r\ndancing with my Emily.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cI was thinking,\xe2\x80\x9d said Steve, with some hesitation, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmind changing for good and all!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cIt was what I was feeling likewise,\xe2\x80\x9d said James.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cI willingly agree to it, if you think we could manage it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cSo do I. But what would the girls say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis my belief,\xe2\x80\x9d said Steve, \xe2\x80\x9cthat they wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t particularly object.\r\nYour Emily clung as close to me as if she already belonged to me, dear\r\ngirl.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cAnd your Olive to me,\xe2\x80\x9d says James. \xe2\x80\x9cI could feel her heart beating\r\nlike a clock.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, they agreed to put it to the girls when they were all four\r\nwalking home together. And they did so. When they parted that night the\r\nexchange was decided on\xe2\x80\x94all having been done under the hot excitement\r\nof that evening\xe2\x80\x99s dancing. Thus it happened that on the following\r\nSunday morning, when the people were sitting in church with mouths wide\r\nopen to hear the names published as they had expected, there was no\r\nsmall amazement to hear them coupled the wrong way, as it seemed. The\r\ncongregation whispered, and thought the parson had made a mistake; till\r\nthey discovered that his reading of the names was verily the true way.\r\nAs they had decided, so they were married, each one to the other\xe2\x80\x99s\r\noriginal property.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, the two couples lived on for a year or two ordinarily enough,\r\ntill the time came when these young people began to grow a little less\r\nwarm to their respective spouses, as is the rule of married life; and\r\nthe two cousins wondered more and more in their hearts what had made\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em so mad at the last moment to marry crosswise as they did, when they\r\nmight have married straight, as was planned by nature, and as they had\r\nfallen in love. \xe2\x80\x99Twas Tony\xe2\x80\x99s party that had done _it_, plain enough,\r\nand they half wished they had never gone there. James, being a quiet,\r\nfireside, perusing man, felt at times a wide gap between himself and\r\nOlive, his wife, who loved riding and driving and out-door jaunts to a\r\ndegree; while Steve, who was always knocking about hither and thither,\r\nhad a very domestic wife, who worked samplers, and made hearthrugs,\r\nscarcely ever wished to cross the threshold, and only drove out with\r\nhim to please him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98However, they said very little about this mismating to any of their\r\nacquaintances, though sometimes Steve would look at James\xe2\x80\x99s wife and\r\nsigh, and James would look at Steve\xe2\x80\x99s wife and do the same. Indeed, at\r\nlast the two men were frank enough towards each other not to mind\r\nmentioning it quietly to themselves, in a long-faced, sorry-smiling,\r\nwhimsical sort of way, and would shake their heads together over their\r\nfoolishness in upsetting a well-considered choice on the strength of an\r\nhour\xe2\x80\x99s fancy in the whirl and wildness of a dance. Still, they were\r\nsensible and honest young fellows enough, and did their best to make\r\nshift with their lot as they had arranged it, and not to repine at what\r\ncould not now be altered or mended.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98So things remained till one fine summer day they went for their yearly\r\nlittle outing together, as they had made it their custom to do for a\r\nlong while past. This year they chose Budmouth-Regis as the place to\r\nspend their holiday in; and off they went in their best clothes at nine\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock in the morning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98When they had reached Budmouth-Regis they walked two and two along the\r\nshore\xe2\x80\x94their new boots going squeakity-squash upon the clammy velvet\r\nsands. I can seem to see \xe2\x80\x99em now! Then they looked at the ships in the\r\nharbour; and then went up to the Look-out; and then had dinner at an\r\ninn; and then again walked two and two, squeakity-squash, upon the\r\nvelvet sands. As evening drew on they sat on one of the public seats\r\nupon the Esplanade, and listened to the band; and then they said \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\r\nshall we do next?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cOf all things,\xe2\x80\x9d said Olive (Mrs. James Hardcome, that is), \xe2\x80\x9cI should\r\nlike to row in the bay! We could listen to the music from the water as\r\nwell as from here, and have the fun of rowing besides.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cThe very thing; so should I,\xe2\x80\x9d says Stephen, his tastes being always\r\nlike hers.\r\n\r\nHere the clerk turned to the curate.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But you, sir, know the rest of the strange particulars of that strange\r\nevening of their lives better than anybody else, having had much of it\r\nfrom their own lips, which I had not; and perhaps you\xe2\x80\x99ll oblige the\r\ngentleman?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Certainly, if it is wished,\xe2\x80\x99 said the curate. And he took up the\r\nclerk\xe2\x80\x99s tale:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Stephen\xe2\x80\x99s wife hated the sea, except from land, and couldn\xe2\x80\x99t bear the\r\nthought of going into a boat. James, too, disliked the water, and said\r\nthat for his part he would much sooner stay on and listen to the band\r\nin the seat they occupied, though he did not wish to stand in his\r\nwife\xe2\x80\x99s way if she desired a row. The end of the discussion was that\r\nJames and his cousin\xe2\x80\x99s wife Emily agreed to remain where they were\r\nsitting and enjoy the music, while they watched the other two hire a\r\nboat just beneath, and take their water-excursion of half an hour or\r\nso, till they should choose to come back and join the sitters on the\r\nEsplanade; when they would all start homeward together.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Nothing could have pleased the other two restless ones better than\r\nthis arrangement; and Emily and James watched them go down to the\r\nboatman below and choose one of the little yellow skiffs, and walk\r\ncarefully out upon the little plank that was laid on trestles to enable\r\nthem to get alongside the craft. They saw Stephen hand Olive in, and\r\ntake his seat facing her; when they were settled they waved their hands\r\nto the couple watching them, and then Stephen took the pair of sculls\r\nand pulled off to the tune beat by the band, she steering through the\r\nother boats skimming about, for the sea was as smooth as glass that\r\nevening, and pleasure-seekers were rowing everywhere.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cHow pretty they look moving on, don\xe2\x80\x99t they?\xe2\x80\x9d said Emily to James (as\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve been assured). \xe2\x80\x9cThey both enjoy it equally. In everything their\r\nlikings are the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s true,\xe2\x80\x9d said James.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cThey would have made a handsome pair if they had married,\xe2\x80\x9d said she.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis a pity we should have parted \xe2\x80\x99em\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t talk of that, James,\xe2\x80\x9d said she. \xe2\x80\x9cFor better or for worse we\r\ndecided to do as we did, and there\xe2\x80\x99s an end of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98They sat on after that without speaking, side by side, and the band\r\nplayed as before; the people strolled up and down; and Stephen and\r\nOlive shrank smaller and smaller as they shot straight out to sea. The\r\ntwo on shore used to relate how they saw Stephen stop rowing a moment,\r\nand take off his coat to get at his work better; but James\xe2\x80\x99s wife sat\r\nquite still in the stern, holding the tiller-ropes by which she steered\r\nthe boat. When they had got very small indeed she turned her head to\r\nshore.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cShe is waving her handkerchief to us,\xe2\x80\x9d said Stephen\xe2\x80\x99s wife, who\r\nthereupon pulled out her own, and waved it as a return signal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The boat\xe2\x80\x99s course had been a little awry while Mrs. James neglected\r\nher steering to wave her handkerchief to her husband and Mrs. Stephen;\r\nbut now the light skiff went straight onward again, and they could soon\r\nsee nothing more of the two figures it contained than Olive\xe2\x80\x99s light\r\nmantle and Stephen\xe2\x80\x99s white shirt sleeves behind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The two on the shore talked on. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Twas very curious\xe2\x80\x94our changing\r\npartners at Tony Kytes\xe2\x80\x99s wedding,\xe2\x80\x9d Emily declared. \xe2\x80\x9cTony was of a\r\nfickle nature by all account, and it really seemed as if his character\r\nhad infected us that night. Which of you two was it that first proposed\r\nnot to marry as we were engaged?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cH\xe2\x80\x99m\xe2\x80\x94I can\xe2\x80\x99t remember at this moment,\xe2\x80\x9d says James. \xe2\x80\x9cWe talked it over,\r\nyou know; and no sooner said than done.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Twas the dancing,\xe2\x80\x9d said she. \xe2\x80\x9cPeople get quite crazy sometimes in a\r\ndance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cThey do,\xe2\x80\x9d he owned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cJames\xe2\x80\x94do you think they care for one another still?\xe2\x80\x9d asks Mrs.\r\nStephen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98James Hardcome mused and admitted that perhaps a little tender feeling\r\nmight flicker up in their hearts for a moment now and then. \xe2\x80\x9cStill,\r\nnothing of any account,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cI sometimes think that Olive is in Steve\xe2\x80\x99s mind a good deal,\xe2\x80\x9d murmurs\r\nMrs. Stephen; \xe2\x80\x9cparticularly when she pleases his fancy by riding past\r\nour window at a gallop on one of the draught-horses . . . I never could\r\ndo anything of that sort; I could never get over my fear of a horse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I am no horseman, though I pretend to be on her account,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nmurmured James Hardcome. \xe2\x80\x9cBut isn\xe2\x80\x99t it almost time for them to turn and\r\nsweep round to the shore, as the other boating folk have done? I wonder\r\nwhat Olive means by steering away straight to the horizon like that?\r\nShe has hardly swerved from a direct line seaward since they started.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cNo doubt they are talking, and don\xe2\x80\x99t think of where they are going,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsuggests Stephen\xe2\x80\x99s wife.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps so,\xe2\x80\x9d said James. \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t know Steve could row like that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cO yes,\xe2\x80\x9d says she. \xe2\x80\x9cHe often comes here on business, and generally has\r\na pull round the bay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cI can hardly see the boat or them,\xe2\x80\x9d says James again; \xe2\x80\x9cand it is\r\ngetting dark.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The heedless pair afloat now formed a mere speck in the films of the\r\ncoming night, which thickened apace, till it completely swallowed up\r\ntheir distant shapes. They had disappeared while still following the\r\nsame straight course away from the world of land-livers, as if they\r\nwere intending to drop over the sea-edge into space, and never return\r\nto earth again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The two on the shore continued to sit on, punctually abiding by their\r\nagreement to remain on the same spot till the others returned. The\r\nEsplanade lamps were lit one by one, the bandsmen folded up their\r\nstands and departed, the yachts in the bay hung out their riding\r\nlights, and the little boats came back to shore one after another,\r\ntheir hirers walking on to the sands by the plank they had climbed to\r\ngo afloat; but among these Stephen and Olive did not appear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a time they are!\xe2\x80\x9d said Emily. \xe2\x80\x9cI am getting quite chilly. I did\r\nnot expect to have to sit so long in the evening air.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Thereupon James Hardcome said that he did not require his overcoat,\r\nand insisted on lending it to her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He wrapped it round Emily\xe2\x80\x99s shoulders.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cThank you, James,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cHow cold Olive must be in that thin\r\njacket!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He said he was thinking so too. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, they are sure to be quite close\r\nat hand by this time, though we can\xe2\x80\x99t see \xe2\x80\x99em. The boats are not all in\r\nyet. Some of the rowers are fond of paddling along the shore to finish\r\nout their hour of hiring.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cShall we walk by the edge of the water,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, \xe2\x80\x9cto see if we can\r\ndiscover them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He assented, reminding her that they must not lose sight of the seat,\r\nlest the belated pair should return and miss them, and be vexed that\r\nthey had not kept the appointment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98They walked a sentry beat up and down the sands immediately opposite\r\nthe seat; and still the others did not come. James Hardcome at last\r\nwent to the boatman, thinking that after all his wife and cousin might\r\nhave come in under shadow of the dusk without being perceived, and\r\nmight have forgotten the appointment at the bench.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cAll in?\xe2\x80\x9d asked James.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cAll but one boat,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lessor. \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t think where that couple\r\nis keeping to. They might run foul of something or other in the dark.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Again Stephen\xe2\x80\x99s wife and Olive\xe2\x80\x99s husband waited, with more and more\r\nanxiety. But no little yellow boat returned. Was it possible they could\r\nhave landed further down the Esplanade?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cIt may have been done to escape paying,\xe2\x80\x9d said the boat-owner. \xe2\x80\x9cBut\r\nthey didn\xe2\x80\x99t look like people who would do that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98James Hardcome knew that he could found no hope on such a reason as\r\nthat. But now, remembering what had been casually discussed between\r\nSteve and himself about their wives from time to time, he admitted for\r\nthe first time the possibility that their old tenderness had been\r\nrevived by their face-to-face position more strongly than either had\r\nanticipated at starting\xe2\x80\x94the excursion having been so obviously\r\nundertaken for the pleasure of the performance only,\xe2\x80\x94and that they had\r\nlanded at some steps he knew of further down toward the pier, to be\r\nlonger alone together.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Still he disliked to harbour the thought, and would not mention its\r\nexistence to his companion. He merely said to her, \xe2\x80\x9cLet us walk further\r\non.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98They did so, and lingered between the boat-stage and the pier till\r\nStephen Hardcome\xe2\x80\x99s wife was uneasy, and was obliged to accept James\xe2\x80\x99s\r\noffered arm. Thus the night advanced. Emily was presently so worn out\r\nby fatigue that James felt it necessary to conduct her home; there was,\r\ntoo, a remote chance that the truants had landed in the harbour on the\r\nother side of the town, or elsewhere, and hastened home in some\r\nunexpected way, in the belief that their consorts would not have waited\r\nso long.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98However, he left a direction in the town that a lookout should be\r\nkept, though this was arranged privately, the bare possibility of an\r\nelopement being enough to make him reticent; and, full of misgivings,\r\nthe two remaining ones hastened to catch the last train out of\r\nBudmouth-Regis; and when they got to Casterbridge drove back to Upper\r\nLongpuddle.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Along this very road as we do now,\xe2\x80\x99 remarked the parish clerk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98To be sure\xe2\x80\x94along this very road,\xe2\x80\x99 said the curate. \xe2\x80\x98However, Stephen\r\nand Olive were not at their homes; neither had entered the village\r\nsince leaving it in the morning. Emily and James Hardcome went to their\r\nrespective dwellings to snatch a hasty night\xe2\x80\x99s rest, and at daylight\r\nthe next morning they drove again to Casterbridge and entered the\r\nBudmouth train, the line being just opened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Nothing had been heard of the couple there during this brief absence.\r\nIn the course of a few hours some young men testified to having seen\r\nsuch a man and woman rowing in a frail hired craft, the head of the\r\nboat kept straight to sea; they had sat looking in each other\xe2\x80\x99s faces\r\nas if they were in a dream, with no consciousness of what they were\r\ndoing, or whither they were steering. It was not till late that day\r\nthat more tidings reached James\xe2\x80\x99s ears. The boat had been found\r\ndrifting bottom upward a long way from land. In the evening the sea\r\nrose somewhat, and a cry spread through the town that two bodies were\r\ncast ashore in Lullstead Bay, several miles to the eastward. They were\r\nbrought to Budmouth, and inspection revealed them to be the missing\r\npair. It was said that they had been found tightly locked in each\r\nother\xe2\x80\x99s arms, his lips upon hers, their features still wrapt in the\r\nsame calm and dream-like repose which had been observed in their\r\ndemeanour as they had glided along.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Neither James nor Emily questioned the original motives of the\r\nunfortunate man and woman in putting to sea. They were both above\r\nsuspicion as to intention. Whatever their mutual feelings might have\r\nled them on to, underhand behaviour was foreign to the nature of\r\neither. Conjecture pictured that they might have fallen into tender\r\nreverie while gazing each into a pair of eyes that had formerly flashed\r\nfor him and her alone, and, unwilling to avow what their mutual\r\nsentiments were, they had continued thus, oblivious of time and space,\r\ntill darkness suddenly overtook them far from land. But nothing was\r\ntruly known. It had been their destiny to die thus. The two halves,\r\nintended by Nature to make the perfect whole, had failed in that result\r\nduring their lives, though \xe2\x80\x9cin their death they were not divided.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nTheir bodies were brought home, and buried on one day. I remember that,\r\non looking round the churchyard while reading the service, I observed\r\nnearly all the parish at their funeral.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It was so, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 said the clerk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The remaining two,\xe2\x80\x99 continued the curate (whose voice had grown husky\r\nwhile relating the lovers\xe2\x80\x99 sad fate), \xe2\x80\x98were a more thoughtful and\r\nfar-seeing, though less romantic, couple than the first. They were now\r\nmutually bereft of a companion, and found themselves by this accident\r\nin a position to fulfil their destiny according to Nature\xe2\x80\x99s plan and\r\ntheir own original and calmly-formed intention. James Hardcome took\r\nEmily to wife in the course of a year and a half; and the marriage\r\nproved in every respect a happy one. I solemnized the service, Hardcome\r\nhaving told me, when he came to give notice of the proposed wedding,\r\nthe story of his first wife\xe2\x80\x99s loss almost word for word as I have told\r\nit to you.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And are they living in Longpuddle still?\xe2\x80\x99 asked the new-comer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98O no, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 interposed the clerk. \xe2\x80\x98James has been dead these dozen\r\nyears, and his mis\xe2\x80\x99ess about six or seven. They had no children.\r\nWilliam Privett used to be their odd man till he died.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ah\xe2\x80\x94William Privett! He dead too?\xe2\x80\x94dear me!\xe2\x80\x99 said the other. \xe2\x80\x98All passed\r\naway!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, sir. William was much older than I. He\xe2\x80\x99d ha\xe2\x80\x99 been over eighty if\r\nhe had lived till now.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98There was something very strange about William\xe2\x80\x99s death\xe2\x80\x94very strange\r\nindeed!\xe2\x80\x99 sighed a melancholy man in the back of the van. It was the\r\nseedsman\xe2\x80\x99s father, who had hitherto kept silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And what might that have been?\xe2\x80\x99 asked Mr. Lackland.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE SUPERSTITIOUS MAN\xe2\x80\x99S STORY\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98William, as you may know, was a curious, silent man; you could feel\r\nwhen he came near \xe2\x80\x99ee; and if he was in the house or anywhere behind\r\nyour back without your seeing him, there seemed to be something clammy\r\nin the air, as if a cellar door was opened close by your elbow. Well,\r\none Sunday, at a time that William was in very good health to all\r\nappearance, the bell that was ringing for church went very heavy all of\r\na sudden; the sexton, who told me o\xe2\x80\x99t, said he\xe2\x80\x99d not known the bell go\r\nso heavy in his hand for years\xe2\x80\x94it was just as if the gudgeons wanted\r\noiling. That was on the Sunday, as I say. During the week after, it\r\nchanced that William\xe2\x80\x99s wife was staying up late one night to finish her\r\nironing, she doing the washing for Mr. and Mrs. Hardcome. Her husband\r\nhad finished his supper and gone to bed as usual some hour or two\r\nbefore. While she ironed she heard him coming down stairs; he stopped\r\nto put on his boots at the stair-foot, where he always left them, and\r\nthen came on into the living-room where she was ironing, passing\r\nthrough it towards the door, this being the only way from the staircase\r\nto the outside of the house. No word was said on either side, William\r\nnot being a man given to much speaking, and his wife being occupied\r\nwith her work. He went out and closed the door behind him. As her\r\nhusband had now and then gone out in this way at night before when\r\nunwell, or unable to sleep for want of a pipe, she took no particular\r\nnotice, and continued at her ironing. This she finished shortly after,\r\nand as he had not come in she waited awhile for him, putting away the\r\nirons and things, and preparing the table for his breakfast in the\r\nmorning. Still he did not return, but supposing him not far off, and\r\nwanting to get to bed herself, tired as she was, she left the door\r\nunbarred and went to the stairs, after writing on the back of the door\r\nwith chalk: _Mind and do the door_ (because he was a forgetful man).\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98To her great surprise, and I might say alarm, on reaching the foot of\r\nthe stairs his boots were standing there as they always stood when he\r\nhad gone to rest; going up to their chamber she found him in bed\r\nsleeping as sound as a rock. How he could have got back again without\r\nher seeing or hearing him was beyond her comprehension. It could only\r\nhave been by passing behind her very quietly while she was bumping with\r\nthe iron. But this notion did not satisfy her: it was surely impossible\r\nthat she should not have seen him come in through a room so small. She\r\ncould not unravel the mystery, and felt very queer and uncomfortable\r\nabout it. However, she would not disturb him to question him then, and\r\nwent to bed herself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He rose and left for his work very early the next morning, before she\r\nwas awake, and she waited his return to breakfast with much anxiety for\r\nan explanation, for thinking over the matter by daylight made it seem\r\nonly the more startling. When he came in to the meal he said, before\r\nshe could put her question, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the meaning of them words chalked\r\non the door?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98She told him, and asked him about his going out the night before.\r\nWilliam declared that he had never left the bedroom after entering it,\r\nhaving in fact undressed, lain down, and fallen asleep directly, never\r\nonce waking till the clock struck five, and he rose up to go to his\r\nlabour.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Betty Privett was as certain in her own mind that he did go out as she\r\nwas of her own existence, and was little less certain that he did not\r\nreturn. She felt too disturbed to argue with him, and let the subject\r\ndrop as though she must have been mistaken. When she was walking down\r\nLongpuddle street later in the day she met Jim Weedle\xe2\x80\x99s daughter Nancy,\r\nand said, \xe2\x80\x9cWell, Nancy, you do look sleepy to-day!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Mrs. Privett,\xe2\x80\x9d says Nancy. \xe2\x80\x9cNow don\xe2\x80\x99t tell anybody, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmind letting you know what the reason o\xe2\x80\x99t is. Last night, being Old\r\nMidsummer Eve, some of us went to church porch, and didn\xe2\x80\x99t get home\r\ntill near one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cDid ye?\xe2\x80\x9d says Mrs. Privett. \xe2\x80\x9cOld Midsummer yesterday was it? Faith I\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t think whe\xe2\x80\x99r \xe2\x80\x99twas Midsummer or Michaelmas; I\xe2\x80\x99d too much work to\r\ndo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cYes. And we were frightened enough, I can tell \xe2\x80\x99ee, by what we saw.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did ye see?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98(You may not remember, sir, having gone off to foreign parts so young,\r\nthat on Midsummer Night it is believed hereabout that the faint shapes\r\nof all the folk in the parish who are going to be at death\xe2\x80\x99s door\r\nwithin the year can be seen entering the church. Those who get over\r\ntheir illness come out again after a while; those that are doomed to\r\ndie do not return.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did you see?\xe2\x80\x9d asked William\xe2\x80\x99s wife.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d says Nancy, backwardly\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cwe needn\xe2\x80\x99t tell what we saw, or who we\r\nsaw.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cYou saw my husband,\xe2\x80\x9d says Betty Privett, in a quiet way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cWell, since you put it so,\xe2\x80\x9d says Nancy, hanging fire, \xe2\x80\x9cwe\xe2\x80\x94thought we\r\ndid see him; but it was darkish, and we was frightened, and of course\r\nit might not have been he.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cNancy, you needn\xe2\x80\x99t mind letting it out, though \xe2\x80\x99tis kept back in\r\nkindness. And he didn\xe2\x80\x99t come out of church again: I know it as well as\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Nancy did not answer yes or no to that, and no more was said. But\r\nthree days after, William Privett was mowing with John Chiles in Mr.\r\nHardcome\xe2\x80\x99s meadow, and in the heat of the day they sat down to eat\r\ntheir bit o\xe2\x80\x99 nunch under a tree, and empty their flagon. Afterwards\r\nboth of \xe2\x80\x99em fell asleep as they sat. John Chiles was the first to wake,\r\nand as he looked towards his fellow-mower he saw one of those great\r\nwhite miller\xe2\x80\x99s-souls as we call \xe2\x80\x99em\xe2\x80\x94that is to say, a miller-moth\xe2\x80\x94come\r\nfrom William\xe2\x80\x99s open mouth while he slept, and fly straight away. John\r\nthought it odd enough, as William had worked in a mill for several\r\nyears when he was a boy. He then looked at the sun, and found by the\r\nplace o\xe2\x80\x99t that they had slept a long while, and as William did not\r\nwake, John called to him and said it was high time to begin work again.\r\nHe took no notice, and then John went up and shook him, and found he\r\nwas dead.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Now on that very day old Philip Hookhorn was down at Longpuddle Spring\r\ndipping up a pitcher of water; and as he turned away, who should he see\r\ncoming down to the spring on the other side but William, looking very\r\npale and odd. This surprised Philip Hookhorn very much, for years\r\nbefore that time William\xe2\x80\x99s little son\xe2\x80\x94his only child\xe2\x80\x94had been drowned\r\nin that spring while at play there, and this had so preyed upon\r\nWilliam\xe2\x80\x99s mind that he\xe2\x80\x99d never been seen near the spring afterwards,\r\nand had been known to go half a mile out of his way to avoid the place.\r\nOn inquiry, it was found that William in body could not have stood by\r\nthe spring, being in the mead two miles off; and it also came out that\r\nthe time at which he was seen at the spring was the very time when he\r\ndied.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98A rather melancholy story,\xe2\x80\x99 observed the emigrant, after a minute\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsilence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, yes. Well, we must take ups and downs together,\xe2\x80\x99 said the\r\nseedsman\xe2\x80\x99s father.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You don\xe2\x80\x99t know, Mr. Lackland, I suppose, what a rum start that was\r\nbetween Andrey Satchel and Jane Vallens and the pa\xe2\x80\x99son and clerk o\xe2\x80\x99\r\nScrimpton?\xe2\x80\x99 said the master-thatcher, a man with a spark of subdued\r\nliveliness in his eye, who had hitherto kept his attention mainly upon\r\nsmall objects a long way ahead, as he sat in front of the van with his\r\nfeet outside. \xe2\x80\x98Theirs was a queerer experience of a pa\xe2\x80\x99son and clerk\r\nthan some folks get, and may cheer \xe2\x80\x99ee up a little after this dampness\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s been flung over yer soul.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe returned one replied that he knew nothing of the history, and\r\nshould be happy to hear it, quite recollecting the personality of the\r\nman Satchel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ah no; this Andrey Satchel is the son of the Satchel that you knew;\r\nthis one has not been married more than two or three years, and \xe2\x80\x99twas\r\nat the time o\xe2\x80\x99 the wedding that the accident happened that I could tell\r\n\xe2\x80\x99ee of, or anybody else here, for that matter.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No, no; you must tell it, neighbour, if anybody,\xe2\x80\x99 said several; a\r\nrequest in which Mr. Lackland joined, adding that the Satchel family\r\nwas one he had known well before leaving home.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99ll just mention, as you be a stranger,\xe2\x80\x99 whispered the carrier to\r\nLackland, \xe2\x80\x98that Christopher\xe2\x80\x99s stories will bear pruning.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe emigrant nodded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, I can soon tell it,\xe2\x80\x99 said the master-thatcher, schooling himself\r\nto a tone of actuality. \xe2\x80\x98Though as it has more to do with the pa\xe2\x80\x99son\r\nand clerk than with Andrey himself, it ought to be told by a better\r\nchurchman than I.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nANDREY SATCHEL AND THE PARSON AND CLERK\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It all arose, you must know, from Andrey being fond of a drop of drink\r\nat that time\xe2\x80\x94though he\xe2\x80\x99s a sober enough man now by all account, so much\r\nthe better for him. Jane, his bride, you see, was somewhat older than\r\nAndrey; how much older I don\xe2\x80\x99t pretend to say; she was not one of our\r\nparish, and the register alone may be able to tell that. But, at any\r\nrate, her being a little ahead of her young man in mortal years,\r\ncoupled with other bodily circumstances\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n(\xe2\x80\x98Ah, poor thing!\xe2\x80\x99 sighed the women.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x94made her very anxious to get the thing done before he changed his\r\nmind; and \xe2\x80\x99twas with a joyful countenance (they say) that she, with\r\nAndrey and his brother and sister-in-law, marched off to church one\r\nNovember morning as soon as \xe2\x80\x99twas day a\xe2\x80\x99most, to be made one with\r\nAndrey for the rest of her life. He had left our place long before it\r\nwas light, and the folks that were up all waved their lanterns at him,\r\nand flung up their hats as he went.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The church of her parish was a mile and more from the houses, and, as\r\nit was a wonderful fine day for the time of year, the plan was that as\r\nsoon as they were married they would make out a holiday by driving\r\nstraight off to Port Bredy, to see the ships and the sea and the\r\nsojers, instead of coming back to a meal at the house of the distant\r\nrelation she lived wi\xe2\x80\x99, and moping about there all the afternoon.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, some folks noticed that Andrey walked with rather wambling steps\r\nto church that morning; the truth o\xe2\x80\x99t was that his nearest neighbour\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nchild had been christened the day before, and Andrey, having stood\r\ngodfather, had stayed all night keeping up the christening, for he had\r\nsaid to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cNot if I live to be thousand shall I again be made a\r\ngodfather one day, and a husband the next, and perhaps a father the\r\nnext, and therefore I\xe2\x80\x99ll make the most of the blessing.\xe2\x80\x9d So that when\r\nhe started from home in the morning he had not been in bed at all. The\r\nresult was, as I say, that when he and his bride-to-be walked up the\r\nchurch to get married, the pa\xe2\x80\x99son (who was a very strict man inside the\r\nchurch, whatever he was outside) looked hard at Andrey, and said, very\r\nsharp:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cHow\xe2\x80\x99s this, my man? You are in liquor. And so early, too. I\xe2\x80\x99m ashamed\r\nof you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that\xe2\x80\x99s true, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d says Andrey. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I can walk straight enough\r\nfor practical purposes. I can walk a chalk line,\xe2\x80\x9d he says (meaning no\r\noffence), \xe2\x80\x9cas well as some other folk: and\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d (getting hotter)\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI reckon\r\nthat if you, Pa\xe2\x80\x99son Billy Toogood, had kept up a christening all night\r\nso thoroughly as I have done, you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be able to stand at all;\r\nd--- me if you would!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98This answer made Pa\xe2\x80\x99son Billy\xe2\x80\x94as they used to call him\xe2\x80\x94rather spitish,\r\nnot to say hot, for he was a warm-tempered man if provoked, and he\r\nsaid, very decidedly: \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I cannot marry you in this state; and I\r\nwill not! Go home and get sober!\xe2\x80\x9d And he slapped the book together like\r\na rat-trap.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then the bride burst out crying as if her heart would break, for very\r\nfear that she would lose Andrey after all her hard work to get him, and\r\nbegged and implored the pa\xe2\x80\x99son to go on with the ceremony. But no.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t be a party to your solemnizing matrimony with a tipsy man,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsays Mr. Toogood. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is not right and decent. I am sorry for you, my\r\nyoung woman, but you\xe2\x80\x99d better go home again. I wonder how you could\r\nthink of bringing him here drunk like this!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cBut if\xe2\x80\x94if he don\xe2\x80\x99t come drunk he won\xe2\x80\x99t come at all, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d she says,\r\nthrough her sobs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t help that,\xe2\x80\x9d says the pa\xe2\x80\x99son; and plead as she might, it did\r\nnot move him. Then she tried him another way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, if you\xe2\x80\x99ll go home, sir, and leave us here, and come back\r\nto the church in an hour or two, I\xe2\x80\x99ll undertake to say that he shall be\r\nas sober as a judge,\xe2\x80\x9d she cries. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll bide here, with your\r\npermission; for if he once goes out of this here church unmarried, all\r\nVan Amburgh\xe2\x80\x99s horses won\xe2\x80\x99t drag him back again!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cVery well,\xe2\x80\x9d says the parson. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll give you two hours, and then I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nreturn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cAnd please, sir, lock the door, so that we can\xe2\x80\x99t escape!\xe2\x80\x9d says she.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d says the parson.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cAnd let nobody know that we are here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The pa\xe2\x80\x99son then took off his clane white surplice, and went away; and\r\nthe others consulted upon the best means for keeping the matter a\r\nsecret, which it was not a very hard thing to do, the place being so\r\nlonely, and the hour so early. The witnesses, Andrey\xe2\x80\x99s brother and\r\nbrother\xe2\x80\x99s wife, neither one o\xe2\x80\x99 which cared about Andrey\xe2\x80\x99s marrying\r\nJane, and had come rather against their will, said they couldn\xe2\x80\x99t wait\r\ntwo hours in that hole of a place, wishing to get home to Longpuddle\r\nbefore dinner-time. They were altogether so crusty that the clerk said\r\nthere was no difficulty in their doing as they wished. They could go\r\nhome as if their brother\xe2\x80\x99s wedding had actually taken place and the\r\nmarried couple had gone onward for their day\xe2\x80\x99s pleasure jaunt to Port\r\nBredy as intended, he, the clerk, and any casual passer-by would act as\r\nwitnesses when the pa\xe2\x80\x99son came back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98This was agreed to, and away Andrey\xe2\x80\x99s relations went, nothing loath,\r\nand the clerk shut the church door and prepared to lock in the couple.\r\nThe bride went up and whispered to him, with her eyes a-streaming\r\nstill.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear good clerk,\xe2\x80\x9d she says, \xe2\x80\x9cif we bide here in the church, folk\r\nmay see us through the winders, and find out what has happened; and\r\n\xe2\x80\x99twould cause such a talk and scandal that I never should get over it:\r\nand perhaps, too, dear Andrey might try to get out and leave me! Will\r\nye lock us up in the tower, my dear good clerk?\xe2\x80\x9d she says. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tole\r\nhim in there if you will.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The clerk had no objection to do this to oblige the poor young woman,\r\nand they toled Andrey into the tower, and the clerk locked \xe2\x80\x99em both up\r\nstraightway, and then went home, to return at the end of the two hours.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Pa\xe2\x80\x99son Toogood had not been long in his house after leaving the church\r\nwhen he saw a gentleman in pink and top-boots ride past his windows,\r\nand with a sudden flash of heat he called to mind that the hounds met\r\nthat day just on the edge of his parish. The pa\xe2\x80\x99son was one who dearly\r\nloved sport, and much he longed to be there.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98In short, except o\xe2\x80\x99 Sundays and at tide-times in the week, Pa\xe2\x80\x99son\r\nBilly was the life o\xe2\x80\x99 the Hunt. \xe2\x80\x99Tis true that he was poor, and that he\r\nrode all of a heap, and that his black mare was rat-tailed and old, and\r\nhis tops older, and all over of one colour, whitey-brown, and full o\xe2\x80\x99\r\ncracks. But he\xe2\x80\x99d been in at the death of three thousand foxes.\r\nAnd\xe2\x80\x94being a bachelor man\xe2\x80\x94every time he went to bed in summer he used to\r\nopen the bed at bottom and crawl up head foremost, to mind \'em of the\r\ncoming winter and the good sport he\xe2\x80\x99d have, and the foxes going to\r\nearth. And whenever there was a christening at the Squire\xe2\x80\x99s, and he had\r\ndinner there afterwards, as he always did, he never failed to christen\r\nthe chiel over again in a bottle of port wine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Now the clerk was the parson\xe2\x80\x99s groom and gardener and jineral manager,\r\nand had just got back to his work in the garden when he, too, saw the\r\nhunting man pass, and presently saw lots more of \xe2\x80\x99em, noblemen and\r\ngentry, and then he saw the hounds, the huntsman, Jim Treadhedge, the\r\nwhipper-in, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t know who besides. The clerk loved going to\r\ncover as frantical as the pa\xe2\x80\x99son, so much so that whenever he saw or\r\nheard the pack he could no more rule his feelings than if they were the\r\nwinds of heaven. He might be bedding, or he might be sowing\xe2\x80\x94all was\r\nforgot. So he throws down his spade and rushes in to the pa\xe2\x80\x99son, who\r\nwas by this time as frantical to go as he.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cThat there mare of yours, sir, do want exercise bad, very bad, this\r\nmorning!\xe2\x80\x9d the clerk says, all of a tremble. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t ye think I\xe2\x80\x99d better\r\ntrot her round the downs for an hour, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cTo be sure, she does want exercise badly. I\xe2\x80\x99ll trot her round\r\nmyself,\xe2\x80\x9d says the parson.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94you\xe2\x80\x99ll trot her yerself? Well, there\xe2\x80\x99s the cob, sir. Really that\r\ncob is getting oncontrollable through biding in a stable so long! If\r\nyou wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t mind my putting on the saddle\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cVery well. Take him out, certainly,\xe2\x80\x9d says the pa\xe2\x80\x99son, never caring\r\nwhat the clerk did so long as he himself could get off immediately. So,\r\nscrambling into his riding-boots and breeches as quick as he could, he\r\nrode off towards the meet, intending to be back in an hour. No sooner\r\nwas he gone than the clerk mounted the cob, and was off after him. When\r\nthe pa\xe2\x80\x99son got to the meet, he found a lot of friends, and was as jolly\r\nas he could be: the hounds found a\xe2\x80\x99most as soon as they threw off, and\r\nthere was great excitement. So, forgetting that he had meant to go back\r\nat once, away rides the pa\xe2\x80\x99son with the rest o\xe2\x80\x99 the hunt, all across\r\nthe fallow ground that lies between Lippet Wood and Green\xe2\x80\x99s Copse; and\r\nas he galloped he looked behind for a moment, and there was the clerk\r\nclose to his heels.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cHa, ha, clerk\xe2\x80\x94you here?\xe2\x80\x9d he says.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir, here be I,\xe2\x80\x9d says t\xe2\x80\x99other.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cFine exercise for the horses!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cAy, sir\xe2\x80\x94hee, hee!\xe2\x80\x9d says the clerk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98So they went on and on, into Green\xe2\x80\x99s Copse, then across to Higher\r\nJirton; then on across this very turnpike-road to Climmerston Ridge,\r\nthen away towards Yalbury Wood: up hill and down dale, like the very\r\nwind, the clerk close to the pa\xe2\x80\x99son, and the pa\xe2\x80\x99son not far from the\r\nhounds. Never was there a finer run knowed with that pack than they had\r\nthat day; and neither pa\xe2\x80\x99son nor clerk thought one word about the\r\nunmarried couple locked up in the church tower waiting to get j\xe2\x80\x99ined.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cThese hosses of yours, sir, will be much improved by this!\xe2\x80\x9d says the\r\nclerk as he rode along, just a neck behind the pa\xe2\x80\x99son. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Twas a happy\r\nthought of your reverent mind to bring \xe2\x80\x99em out to-day. Why, it may be\r\nfrosty in a day or two, and then the poor things mid not be able to\r\nleave the stable for weeks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cThey may not, they may not, it is true. A merciful man is merciful to\r\nhis beast,\xe2\x80\x9d says the pa\xe2\x80\x99son.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cHee, hee!\xe2\x80\x9d says the clerk, glancing sly into the pa\xe2\x80\x99son\xe2\x80\x99s eye.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cHa, ha!\xe2\x80\x9d says the pa\xe2\x80\x99son, a-glancing back into the clerk\xe2\x80\x99s. \xe2\x80\x9cHalloo!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe shouts, as he sees the fox break cover at that moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cHalloo!\xe2\x80\x9d cries the clerk. \xe2\x80\x9cThere he goes! Why, dammy, there\xe2\x80\x99s two\r\nfoxes\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cHush, clerk, hush! Don\xe2\x80\x99t let me hear that word again! Remember our\r\ncalling.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cTrue, sir, true. But really, good sport do carry away a man so, that\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99s apt to forget his high persuasion!\xe2\x80\x9d And the next minute the corner\r\nof the clerk\xe2\x80\x99s eye shot again into the corner of the pa\xe2\x80\x99son\xe2\x80\x99s, and the\r\npa\xe2\x80\x99son\xe2\x80\x99s back again to the clerk\xe2\x80\x99s. \xe2\x80\x9cHee, hee!\xe2\x80\x9d said the clerk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cHa, ha!\xe2\x80\x9d said Pa\xe2\x80\x99son Toogood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cAh, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d says the clerk again, \xe2\x80\x9cthis is better than crying Amen to\r\nyour Ever-and-ever on a winter\xe2\x80\x99s morning!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cYes, indeed, clerk! To everything there\xe2\x80\x99s a season,\xe2\x80\x9d says Pa\xe2\x80\x99son\r\nToogood, quite pat, for he was a learned Christian man when he liked,\r\nand had chapter and ve\xe2\x80\x99se at his tongue\xe2\x80\x99s end, as a pa\xe2\x80\x99son should.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98At last, late in the day, the hunting came to an end by the fox\r\nrunning into a\xe2\x80\x99 old woman\xe2\x80\x99s cottage, under her table, and up the\r\nclock-case. The pa\xe2\x80\x99son and clerk were among the first in at the death,\r\ntheir faces a-staring in at the old woman\xe2\x80\x99s winder, and the clock\r\nstriking as he\xe2\x80\x99d never been heard to strik\xe2\x80\x99 before. Then came the\r\nquestion of finding their way home.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Neither the pa\xe2\x80\x99son nor the clerk knowed how they were going to do\r\nthis, for their beasts were wellnigh tired down to the ground. As soon as ever the horses\r\nhad been stabled and fed, and the pa\xe2\x80\x99son and clerk had had a bit and a\r\nsup theirselves, they went to bed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Next morning when Pa\xe2\x80\x99son Toogood was at breakfast, thinking of the\r\nglorious sport he\xe2\x80\x99d had the day before, the clerk came in a hurry to\r\nthe door and asked to see him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cIt has just come into my mind, sir, that we\xe2\x80\x99ve forgot all about the\r\ncouple that we was to have married yesterday!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The half-chawed victuals dropped from the pa\xe2\x80\x99son\xe2\x80\x99s mouth as if he\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nbeen shot. \xe2\x80\x9cBless my soul,\xe2\x80\x9d says he, \xe2\x80\x9cso we have! How very awkward!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cIt is, sir; very. Perhaps we\xe2\x80\x99ve ruined the \xe2\x80\x99ooman!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cAh\xe2\x80\x94to be sure\xe2\x80\x94I remember! She ought to have been married before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cIf anything has happened to her up in that there tower, and no doctor\r\nor nuss\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n(\xe2\x80\x98Ah\xe2\x80\x94poor thing!\xe2\x80\x99 sighed the women.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99twill be a quarter-sessions matter for us, not to speak of the\r\ndisgrace to the Church!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cGood God, clerk, don\xe2\x80\x99t drive me wild!\xe2\x80\x9d says the pa\xe2\x80\x99son. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy the hell\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t I marry \xe2\x80\x99em, drunk or sober!\xe2\x80\x9d (Pa\xe2\x80\x99sons used to cuss in them days\r\nlike plain honest men.) \xe2\x80\x9cHave you been to the church to see what\r\nhappened to them, or inquired in the village?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cNot I, sir! You could have knocked me down\r\nwith a sparrer\xe2\x80\x99s feather when I thought o\xe2\x80\x99t, sir; I assure \xe2\x80\x99ee you\r\ncould!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, the parson jumped up from his breakfast, and together they went\r\noff to the church.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not at all likely that they are there now,\xe2\x80\x9d says Mr. Toogood,\r\nas they went; \xe2\x80\x9cand indeed I hope they are not. But to more godly business!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98They went on into the church, and unlocked the tower stairs, and\r\nimmediately poor Jane and Andrey busted out like starved mice from a\r\ncupboard, Andrey limp and sober enough now, and his bride pale and\r\ncold, but otherwise as usual.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cWhat,\xe2\x80\x9d says the pa\xe2\x80\x99son, with a great breath of relief, \xe2\x80\x9cyou haven\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbeen here ever since?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cYes, we have, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d says the bride, sinking down upon a seat in her\r\nweakness. \xe2\x80\x9cNot a morsel, wet or dry, have we had since!
