I won\xe2\x80\x99t bring disgrace on my name and\r\nyours, my dear.\xe2\x80\x9d And so we waited and waited, and walked round and\r\nround; but never did you come till now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cTo my regret!\xe2\x80\x9d says the parson. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, then, we will soon get it\r\nover.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I should like some victuals,\xe2\x80\x9d said Andrey, \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99twould gie me courage\r\nif it is only a crust o\xe2\x80\x99 bread and a\xe2\x80\x99 onion; for I am that leery that I\r\ncan feel my stomach rubbing against my backbone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cI think we had better get it done,\xe2\x80\x9d said the bride, a bit anxious in\r\nmanner; \xe2\x80\x9csince we are all here convenient, too!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Andrey gave way about the victuals, and the clerk called in a second\r\nwitness who wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be likely to gossip about it, and soon the knot\r\nwas tied, and the bride looked smiling and calm forthwith, and Andrey\r\nlimper than ever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cNow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Pa\xe2\x80\x99son Toogood, \xe2\x80\x9cyou two must come to my house, and have a\r\ngood lining put to your insides before you go a step further.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98They were very glad of the offer, and went out of the churchyard by\r\none path while the pa\xe2\x80\x99son and clerk went out by the other, and so did\r\nnot attract notice, it being still early. They entered the rectory as\r\nif they\xe2\x80\x99d just come back from their trip to Port Bredy; and then they\r\nknocked in the victuals and drink till they could hold no more.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It was a long while before the story of what they had gone through was\r\nknown, but it was talked of in time, and they themselves laugh over it\r\nnow; though what Jane got for her pains was no great bargain after all.\r\n\xe2\x80\x99Tis true she saved her name.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Was that the same Andrey who went to the squire\xe2\x80\x99s house as one of the\r\nChristmas fiddlers?\xe2\x80\x99 asked the seedsman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No, no,\xe2\x80\x99 replied Mr. Profitt, the schoolmaster. \xe2\x80\x98It was his father did\r\nthat. Ay, it was all owing to his being such a man for eating and\r\ndrinking.\xe2\x80\x99 Finding that he had the ear of the audience, the\r\nschoolmaster continued without delay:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nOLD ANDREY\xe2\x80\x99S EXPERIENCE AS A MUSICIAN\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I was one of the choir-boys at that time, and we and the players were\r\nto appear at the manor-house as usual that Christmas week, to play and\r\nsing in the hall to the squire\xe2\x80\x99s people and visitors (among \xe2\x80\x99em being\r\nthe archdeacon, Lord and Lady Baxby, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t know who); afterwards\r\ngoing, as we always did, to have a good supper in the servants\xe2\x80\x99 hall.\r\nAndrew knew this was the custom, and meeting us when we were starting\r\nto go, he said to us: \xe2\x80\x9cLord, how I should like to join in that meal of\r\nbeef, and turkey, and plum-pudding, and ale, that you happy ones be\r\ngoing to just now! One more or less will make no difference to the\r\nsquire. I am too old to pass as a singing boy, and too bearded to pass\r\nas a singing girl; can ye lend me a fiddle, neighbours, that I may come\r\nwith ye as a bandsman?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, we didn\xe2\x80\x99t like to be hard upon him, and lent him an old one,\r\nthough Andrew knew no more of music than the Cerne Giant; and armed\r\nwith the instrument he walked up to the squire\xe2\x80\x99s house with the others\r\nof us at the time appointed, and went in boldly, his fiddle under his\r\narm. He made himself as natural as he could in opening the music-books\r\nand moving the candles to the best points for throwing light upon the\r\nnotes; and all went well till we had played and sung \xe2\x80\x9cWhile shepherds\r\nwatch,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cStar, arise,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cHark the glad sound.\xe2\x80\x9d Then the squire\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmother, a tall gruff old lady, who was much interested in church-music,\r\nsaid quite unexpectedly to Andrew: \xe2\x80\x9cMy man, I see you don\xe2\x80\x99t play your\r\ninstrument with the rest. How is that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Every one of the choir was ready to sink into the earth with concern\r\nat the fix Andrew was in. We could see that he had fallen into a cold\r\nsweat, and how he would get out of it we did not know.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve had a misfortune, mem,\xe2\x80\x9d he says, bowing as meek as a child.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cComing along the road I fell down and broke my bow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I am sorry to hear that,\xe2\x80\x9d says she. \xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t it be mended?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, mem,\xe2\x80\x9d says Andrew. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Twas broke all to splinters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll see what I can do for you,\xe2\x80\x9d says she.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And then it seemed all over, and we played \xe2\x80\x9cRejoice, ye drowsy mortals\r\nall,\xe2\x80\x9d in D and two sharps. But no sooner had we got through it than she\r\nsays to Andrew,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve sent up into the attic, where we have some old musical\r\ninstruments, and found a bow for you.\xe2\x80\x9d And she hands the bow to poor\r\nwretched Andrew, who didn\xe2\x80\x99t even know which end to take hold of. \xe2\x80\x9cNow\r\nwe shall have the full accompaniment,\xe2\x80\x9d says she.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Andrew\xe2\x80\x99s face looked as if it were made of rotten apple as he stood in\r\nthe circle of players in front of his book; for if there was one person\r\nin the parish that everybody was afraid of, \xe2\x80\x99twas this hook-nosed old\r\nlady. However, by keeping a little behind the next man he managed to\r\nmake pretence of beginning, sawing away with his bow without letting it\r\ntouch the strings, so that it looked as if he were driving into the\r\ntune with heart and soul. \xe2\x80\x99Tis a question if he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have got\r\nthrough all right if one of the squire\xe2\x80\x99s visitors (no other than the\r\narchdeacon) hadn\xe2\x80\x99t noticed that he held the fiddle upside down, the nut\r\nunder his chin, and the tail-piece in his hand; and they began to crowd\r\nround him, thinking \xe2\x80\x99twas some new way of performing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98This revealed everything; the squire\xe2\x80\x99s mother had Andrew turned out of\r\nthe house as a vile impostor, and there was great interruption to the\r\nharmony of the proceedings, the squire declaring he should have notice\r\nto leave his cottage that day fortnight. However, when we got to the\r\nservants\xe2\x80\x99 hall there sat Andrew, who had been let in at the back door\r\nby the orders of the squire\xe2\x80\x99s wife, after being turned out at the front\r\nby the orders of the squire, and nothing more was heard about his\r\nleaving his cottage. But Andrew never performed in public as a musician\r\nafter that night; and now he\xe2\x80\x99s dead and gone, poor man, as we all shall\r\nbe!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I had quite forgotten the old choir, with their fiddles and\r\nbass-viols,\xe2\x80\x99 said the home-comer, musingly. \xe2\x80\x98Are they still going on\r\nthe same as of old?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Bless the man!\xe2\x80\x99 said Christopher Twink, the master-thatcher; \xe2\x80\x98why,\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99ve been done away with these twenty year. A young teetotaler plays\r\nthe organ in church now, and plays it very well; though \xe2\x80\x99tis not quite\r\nsuch good music as in old times, because the organ is one of them that\r\ngo with a winch, and the young teetotaler says he can\xe2\x80\x99t always throw\r\nthe proper feeling into the tune without wellnigh working his arms\r\noff.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why did they make the change, then?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, partly because of fashion, partly because the old musicians got\r\ninto a sort of scrape. A terrible scrape \xe2\x80\x99twas too\xe2\x80\x94wasn\xe2\x80\x99t it, John? I\r\nshall never forget it\xe2\x80\x94never! They lost their character as officers of\r\nthe church as complete as if they\xe2\x80\x99d never had any character at all.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That was very bad for them.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes.\xe2\x80\x99 The master-thatcher attentively regarded past times as if they\r\nlay about a mile off, and went on:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nABSENT-MINDEDNESS IN A PARISH CHOIR\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It happened on Sunday after Christmas\xe2\x80\x94the last Sunday ever they played\r\nin Longpuddle church gallery, as it turned out, though they didn\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nit then. As you may know, sir, the players formed a very good\r\nband\xe2\x80\x94almost as good as the Mellstock parish players that were led by\r\nthe Dewys; and that\xe2\x80\x99s saying a great deal. There was Nicholas\r\nPuddingcome, the leader, with the first fiddle; there was Timothy\r\nThomas, the bass-viol man; John Biles, the tenor fiddler; Dan\xe2\x80\x99l\r\nHornhead, with the serpent; Robert Dowdle, with the clarionet; and Mr.\r\nNicks, with the oboe\xe2\x80\x94all sound and powerful musicians, and\r\nstrong-winded men\xe2\x80\x94they that blowed. For that reason they were very much\r\nin demand Christmas week for little reels and dancing parties; for they\r\ncould turn a jig or a hornpipe out of hand as well as ever they could\r\nturn out a psalm, and perhaps better, not to speak irreverent. In\r\nshort, one half-hour they could be playing a Christmas carol in the\r\nsquire\xe2\x80\x99s hall to the ladies and gentlemen, and drinking tea and coffee\r\nwith \xe2\x80\x99em as modest as saints; and the next, at The Tinker\xe2\x80\x99s Arms,\r\nblazing away like wild horses with the \xe2\x80\x9cDashing White Sergeant\xe2\x80\x9d to nine\r\ncouple of dancers and more, and swallowing rum-and-cider hot as flame.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, this Christmas they\xe2\x80\x99d been out to one rattling randy after\r\nanother every night, and had got next to no sleep at all. Then came the\r\nSunday after Christmas, their fatal day. \xe2\x80\x99Twas so mortal cold that year\r\nthat they could hardly sit in the gallery; for though the congregation\r\ndown in the body of the church had a stove to keep off the frost, the\r\nplayers in the gallery had nothing at all. So Nicholas said at morning\r\nservice, when \xe2\x80\x99twas freezing an inch an hour, \xe2\x80\x9cPlease the Lord I won\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nstand this numbing weather no longer: this afternoon we\xe2\x80\x99ll have\r\nsomething in our insides to make us warm, if it cost a king\xe2\x80\x99s ransom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98So he brought a gallon of hot brandy and beer, ready mixed, to church\r\nwith him in the afternoon, and by keeping the jar well wrapped up in\r\nTimothy Thomas\xe2\x80\x99s bass-viol bag it kept drinkably warm till they wanted\r\nit, which was just a thimbleful in the Absolution, and another after\r\nthe Creed, and the remainder at the beginning o\xe2\x80\x99 the sermon. When\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99d had the last pull they felt quite comfortable and warm, and as\r\nthe sermon went on\xe2\x80\x94most unfortunately for \xe2\x80\x99em it was a long one that\r\nafternoon\xe2\x80\x94they fell asleep, every man jack of \xe2\x80\x99em; and there they slept\r\non as sound as rocks.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x99Twas a very dark afternoon, and by the end of the sermon all you\r\ncould see of the inside of the church were the pa\xe2\x80\x99son\xe2\x80\x99s two candles\r\nalongside of him in the pulpit, and his spaking face behind \xe2\x80\x99em. The\r\nsermon being ended at last, the pa\xe2\x80\x99son gie\xe2\x80\x99d out the Evening Hymn. But\r\nno choir set about sounding up the tune, and the people began to turn\r\ntheir heads to learn the reason why, and then Levi Limpet, a boy who\r\nsat in the gallery, nudged Timothy and Nicholas, and said, \xe2\x80\x9cBegin!\r\nbegin!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cHey? what?\xe2\x80\x9d says Nicholas, starting up; and the church being so dark\r\nand his head so muddled he thought he was at the party they had played\r\nat all the night before, and away he went, bow and fiddle, at \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nDevil among the Tailors,\xe2\x80\x9d the favourite jig of our neighbourhood at\r\nthat time. The rest of the band, being in the same state of mind and\r\nnothing doubting, followed their leader with all their strength,\r\naccording to custom. They poured out that there tune till the lower\r\nbass notes of \xe2\x80\x9cThe Devil among the Tailors\xe2\x80\x9d made the cobwebs in the\r\nroof shiver like ghosts; then Nicholas, seeing nobody moved, shouted\r\nout as he scraped (in his usual commanding way at dances when the folk\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t know the figures), \xe2\x80\x9cTop couples cross hands! And when I make the\r\nfiddle squeak at the end, every man kiss his pardner under the\r\nmistletoe!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The boy Levi was so frightened that he bolted down the gallery stairs\r\nand out homeward like lightning. The pa\xe2\x80\x99son\xe2\x80\x99s hair fairly stood on end\r\nwhen he heard the evil tune raging through the church, and thinking the\r\nchoir had gone crazy he held up his hand and said: \xe2\x80\x9cStop, stop, stop!\r\nStop, stop! What\xe2\x80\x99s this?\xe2\x80\x9d But they didn\xe2\x80\x99t hear\xe2\x80\x99n for the noise of their\r\nown playing, and the more he called the louder they played.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then the folks came out of their pews, wondering down to the ground,\r\nand saying: \xe2\x80\x9cWhat do they mean by such wickedness! We shall be consumed\r\nlike Sodom and Gomorrah!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then the squire came out of his pew lined wi\xe2\x80\x99 green baize, where lots\r\nof lords and ladies visiting at the house were worshipping along with\r\nhim, and went and stood in front of the gallery, and shook his fist in\r\nthe musicians\xe2\x80\x99 faces, saying, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat! In this reverent edifice! What!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And at last they heard\xe2\x80\x99n through their playing, and stopped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cNever such an insulting, disgraceful thing\xe2\x80\x94never!\xe2\x80\x9d says the squire,\r\nwho couldn\xe2\x80\x99t rule his passion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cNever!\xe2\x80\x9d says the pa\xe2\x80\x99son, who had come down and stood beside him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cNot if the Angels of Heaven,\xe2\x80\x9d says the squire (he was a wickedish\r\nman, the squire was, though now for once he happened to be on the\r\nLord\xe2\x80\x99s side)\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cnot if the Angels of Heaven come down,\xe2\x80\x9d he says, \xe2\x80\x9cshall\r\none of you villanous players ever sound a note in this church again;\r\nfor the insult to me, and my family, and my visitors, and God Almighty,\r\nthat you\xe2\x80\x99ve a-perpetrated this afternoon!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then the unfortunate church band came to their senses, and remembered\r\nwhere they were; and \xe2\x80\x99twas a sight to see Nicholas Pudding come and\r\nTimothy Thomas and John Biles creep down the gallery stairs with their\r\nfiddles under their arms, and poor Dan\xe2\x80\x99l Hornhead with his serpent, and\r\nRobert Dowdle with his clarionet, all looking as little as ninepins;\r\nand out they went. The pa\xe2\x80\x99son might have forgi\xe2\x80\x99ed \xe2\x80\x99em when he learned\r\nthe truth o\xe2\x80\x99t, but the squire would not. That very week he sent for a\r\nbarrel-organ that would play two-and-twenty new psalm-tunes, so exact\r\nand particular that, however sinful inclined you was, you could play\r\nnothing but psalm-tunes whatsomever. He had a really respectable man to\r\nturn the winch, as I said, and the old players played no more.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And, of course, my old acquaintance, the annuitant, Mrs. Winter, who\r\nalways seemed to have something on her mind, is dead and gone?\xe2\x80\x99 said\r\nthe home-comer, after a long silence.\r\n\r\nNobody in the van seemed to recollect the name.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98O yes, she must be dead long since: she was seventy when I as a child\r\nknew her,\xe2\x80\x99 he added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I can recollect Mrs. Winter very well, if nobody else can,\xe2\x80\x99 said the\r\naged groceress. \xe2\x80\x98Yes, she\xe2\x80\x99s been dead these five-and-twenty year at\r\nleast. You knew what it was upon her mind, sir, that gave her that\r\nhollow-eyed look, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It had something to do with a son of hers, I think I once was told.\r\nBut I was too young to know particulars.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe groceress sighed as she conjured up a vision of days long past.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes,\xe2\x80\x99 she murmured, \xe2\x80\x98it had all to do with a son.\xe2\x80\x99 Finding that the\r\nvan was still in a listening mood, she spoke on:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE WINTERS AND THE PALMLEYS\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98To go back to the beginning\xe2\x80\x94if one must\xe2\x80\x94there were two women in the\r\nparish when I was a child, who were to a certain extent rivals in good\r\nlooks. Never mind particulars, but in consequence of this they were at\r\ndaggers-drawn, and they did not love each other any better when one of\r\nthem tempted the other\xe2\x80\x99s lover away from her and married him. He was a\r\nyoung man of the name of Winter, and in due time they had a son.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The other woman did not marry for many years: but when she was about\r\nthirty a quiet man named Palmley asked her to be his wife, and she\r\naccepted him. You don\xe2\x80\x99t mind when the Palmleys were Longpuddle folk,\r\nbut I do well. She had a son also, who was, of course, nine or ten\r\nyears younger than the son of the first. The child proved to be of\r\nrather weak intellect, though his mother loved him as the apple of her\r\neye.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98This woman\xe2\x80\x99s husband died when the child was eight years old, and left\r\nhis widow and boy in poverty. Her former rival, also a widow now, but\r\nfairly well provided for, offered for pity\xe2\x80\x99s sake to take the child as\r\nerrand-boy, small as he was, her own son, Jack, being hard upon\r\nseventeen. Her poor neighbour could do no better than let the child go\r\nthere. And to the richer woman\xe2\x80\x99s house little Palmley straightway went.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, in some way or other\xe2\x80\x94how, it was never exactly known\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nthriving woman, Mrs. Winter, sent the little boy with a message to the\r\nnext village one December day, much against his will. It was getting\r\ndark, and the child prayed to be allowed not to go, because he would be\r\nafraid coming home. But the mistress insisted, more out of\r\nthoughtlessness than cruelty, and the child went. On his way back he\r\nhad to pass through Yalbury Wood, and something came out from behind a\r\ntree and frightened him into fits. The child was quite ruined by it; he\r\nbecame quite a drivelling idiot, and soon afterward died.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then the other woman had nothing left to live for, and vowed vengeance\r\nagainst that rival who had first won away her lover, and now had been\r\nthe cause of her bereavement. This last affliction was certainly not\r\nintended by her thriving acquaintance, though it must be owned that\r\nwhen it was done she seemed but little concerned. Whatever vengeance\r\npoor Mrs. Palmley felt, she had no opportunity of carrying it out, and\r\ntime might have softened her feelings into forgetfulness of her\r\nsupposed wrongs as she dragged on her lonely life. So matters stood\r\nwhen, a year after the death of the child, Mrs. Palmley\xe2\x80\x99s niece, who\r\nhad been born and bred in the city of Exonbury, came to live with her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98This young woman\xe2\x80\x94Miss Harriet Palmley\xe2\x80\x94was a proud and handsome girl,\r\nvery well brought up, and more stylish and genteel than the people of\r\nour village, as was natural, considering where she came from. She\r\nregarded herself as much above Mrs. Winter and her son in position as\r\nMrs. Winter and her son considered themselves above poor Mrs. Palmley.\r\nBut love is an unceremonious thing, and what in the world should happen\r\nbut that young Jack Winter must fall wofully and wildly in love with\r\nHarriet Palmley almost as soon as he saw her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98She, being better educated than he, and caring nothing for the village\r\nnotion of his mother\xe2\x80\x99s superiority to her aunt, did not give him much\r\nencouragement. But Longpuddle being no very large world, the two could\r\nnot help seeing a good deal of each other while she was staying there,\r\nand, disdainful young woman as she was, she did seem to take a little\r\npleasure in his attentions and advances.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98One day when they were picking apples together, he asked her to marry\r\nhim. She had not expected anything so practical as that at so early a\r\ntime, and was led by her surprise into a half-promise; at any rate she\r\ndid not absolutely refuse him, and accepted some little presents that\r\nhe made her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But he saw that her view of him was rather as a simple village lad\r\nthan as a young man to look up to, and he felt that he must do\r\nsomething bold to secure her. So he said one day, \xe2\x80\x9cI am going away, to\r\ntry to get into a better position than I can get here.\xe2\x80\x9d In two or three\r\nweeks he wished her good-bye, and went away to Monksbury, to\r\nsuperintend a farm, with a view to start as a farmer himself; and from\r\nthere he wrote regularly to her, as if their marriage were an\r\nunderstood thing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Now Harriet liked the young man\xe2\x80\x99s presents and the admiration of his\r\neyes; but on paper he was less attractive to her. Her mother had been a\r\nschool-mistress, and Harriet had besides a natural aptitude for\r\npen-and-ink work, in days when to be a ready writer was not such a\r\ncommon thing as it is now, and when actual handwriting was valued as an\r\naccomplishment in itself. Jack Winter\xe2\x80\x99s performances in the shape of\r\nlove-letters quite jarred her city nerves and her finer taste, and when\r\nshe answered one of them, in the lovely running hand that she took such\r\npride in, she very strictly and loftily bade him to practise with a pen\r\nand spelling-book if he wished to please her. Whether he listened to\r\nher request or not nobody knows, but his letters did not improve. He\r\nventured to tell her in his clumsy way that if her heart were more warm\r\ntowards him she would not be so nice about his handwriting and\r\nspelling; which indeed was true enough.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, in Jack\xe2\x80\x99s absence the weak flame that had been set alight in\r\nHarriet\xe2\x80\x99s heart soon sank low, and at last went out altogether. He\r\nwrote and wrote, and begged and prayed her to give a reason for her\r\ncoldness; and then she told him plainly that she was town born, and he\r\nwas not sufficiently well educated to please her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Jack Winter\xe2\x80\x99s want of pen-and-ink training did not make him less\r\nthin-skinned than others; in fact, he was terribly tender and touchy\r\nabout anything. This reason that she gave for finally throwing him over\r\ngrieved him, shamed him, and mortified him more than can be told in\r\nthese times, the pride of that day in being able to write with\r\nbeautiful flourishes, and the sorrow at not being able to do so, raging\r\nso high. Jack replied to her with an angry note, and then she hit back\r\nwith smart little stings, telling him how many words he had misspelt in\r\nhis last letter, and declaring again that this alone was sufficient\r\njustification for any woman to put an end to an understanding with him.\r\nHer husband must be a better scholar.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He bore her rejection of him in silence, but his suffering was\r\nsharp\xe2\x80\x94all the sharper in being untold. She communicated with Jack no\r\nmore; and as his reason for going out into the world had been only to\r\nprovide a home worthy of her, he had no further object in planning such\r\na home now that she was lost to him. He therefore gave up the farming\r\noccupation by which he had hoped to make himself a master-farmer, and\r\nleft the spot to return to his mother.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98As soon as he got back to Longpuddle he found that Harriet had already\r\nlooked wi\xe2\x80\x99 favour upon another lover. He was a young road-contractor,\r\nand Jack could not but admit that his rival was both in manners and\r\nscholarship much ahead of him. Indeed, a more sensible match for the\r\nbeauty who had been dropped into the village by fate could hardly have\r\nbeen found than this man, who could offer her so much better a chance\r\nthan Jack could have done, with his uncertain future and narrow\r\nabilities for grappling with the world. The fact was so clear to him\r\nthat he could hardly blame her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98One day by accident Jack saw on a scrap of paper the handwriting of\r\nHarriet\xe2\x80\x99s new beloved. It was flowing like a stream, well spelt, the\r\nwork of a man accustomed to the ink-bottle and the dictionary, of a man\r\nalready called in the parish a good scholar. And then it struck all of\r\na sudden into Jack\xe2\x80\x99s mind what a contrast the letters of this young man\r\nmust make to his own miserable old letters, and how ridiculous they\r\nmust make his lines appear. He groaned and wished he had never written\r\nto her, and wondered if she had ever kept his poor performances.\r\nPossibly she had kept them, for women are in the habit of doing that,\r\nhe thought, and whilst they were in her hands there was always a chance\r\nof his honest, stupid love-assurances to her being joked over by\r\nHarriet with her present lover, or by anybody who should accidentally\r\nuncover them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The nervous, moody young man could not bear the thought of it, and at\r\nlength decided to ask her to return them, as was proper when\r\nengagements were broken off. He was some hours in framing, copying, and\r\nrecopying the short note in which he made his request, and having\r\nfinished it he sent it to her house. His messenger came back with the\r\nanswer, by word of mouth, that Miss Palmley bade him say she should not\r\npart with what was hers, and wondered at his boldness in troubling her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Jack was much affronted at this, and determined to go for his letters\r\nhimself. He chose a time when he knew she was at home, and knocked and\r\nwent in without much ceremony; for though Harriet was so high and\r\nmighty, Jack had small respect for her aunt, Mrs. Palmley, whose little\r\nchild had been his boot-cleaner in earlier days. Harriet was in the\r\nroom, this being the first time they had met since she had jilted him.\r\nHe asked for his letters with a stern and bitter look at her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98At first she said he might have them for all that she cared, and took\r\nthem out of the bureau where she kept them. Then she glanced over the\r\noutside one of the packet, and suddenly altering her mind, she told him\r\nshortly that his request was a silly one, and slipped the letters into\r\nher aunt\xe2\x80\x99s work-box, which stood open on the table, locking it, and\r\nsaying with a bantering laugh that of course she thought it best to\r\nkeep \xe2\x80\x99em, since they might be useful to produce as evidence that she\r\nhad good cause for declining to marry him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He blazed up hot. \xe2\x80\x9cGive me those letters!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThey are mine!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cNo, they are not,\xe2\x80\x9d she replied; \xe2\x80\x9cthey are mine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cWhos\xe2\x80\x99ever they are I want them back,\xe2\x80\x9d says he. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want to be\r\nmade sport of for my penmanship: you\xe2\x80\x99ve another young man now! he has\r\nyour confidence, and you pour all your tales into his ear. You\xe2\x80\x99ll be\r\nshowing them to him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps,\xe2\x80\x9d said my lady Harriet, with calm coolness, like the\r\nheartless woman that she was.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Her manner so maddened him that he made a step towards the work-box,\r\nbut she snatched it up, locked it in the bureau, and turned upon him\r\ntriumphant. For a moment he seemed to be going to wrench the key of the\r\nbureau out of her hand; but he stopped himself, and swung round upon\r\nhis heel and went away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98When he was out-of-doors alone, and it got night, he walked about\r\nrestless, and stinging with the sense of being beaten at all points by\r\nher. He could not help fancying her telling her new lover or her\r\nacquaintances of this scene with himself, and laughing with them over\r\nthose poor blotted, crooked lines of his that he had been so anxious to\r\nobtain. As the evening passed on he worked himself into a dogged\r\nresolution to have them back at any price, come what might.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98At the dead of night he came out of his mother\xe2\x80\x99s house by the back\r\ndoor, and creeping through the garden hedge went along the field\r\nadjoining till he reached the back of her aunt\xe2\x80\x99s dwelling. The moon\r\nstruck bright and flat upon the walls, \xe2\x80\x99twas said, and every shiny leaf\r\nof the creepers was like a little looking-glass in the rays. From long\r\nacquaintance Jack knew the arrangement and position of everything in\r\nMrs. Palmley\xe2\x80\x99s house as well as in his own mother\xe2\x80\x99s. The back window\r\nclose to him was a casement with little leaded squares, as it is to\r\nthis day, and was, as now, one of two lighting the sitting-room. The\r\nother, being in front, was closed up with shutters, but this back one\r\nhad not even a blind, and the moonlight as it streamed in showed every\r\narticle of the furniture to him outside. To the right of the room is\r\nthe fireplace, as you may remember; to the left was the bureau at that\r\ntime; inside the bureau was Harriet\xe2\x80\x99s work-box, as he supposed (though\r\nit was really her aunt\xe2\x80\x99s), and inside the work-box were his letters.\r\nWell, he took out his pocket-knife, and without noise lifted the\r\nleading of one of the panes, so that he could take out the glass, and\r\nputting his hand through the hole he unfastened the casement, and\r\nclimbed in through the opening. All the household\xe2\x80\x94that is to say, Mrs.\r\nPalmley, Harriet, and the little maid-servant\xe2\x80\x94were asleep. Jack went\r\nstraight to the bureau, so he said, hoping it might have been\r\nunfastened again\xe2\x80\x94it not being kept locked in ordinary\xe2\x80\x94but Harriet had\r\nnever unfastened it since she secured her letters there the day before.\r\nJack told afterward how he thought of her asleep upstairs, caring\r\nnothing for him, and of the way she had made sport of him and of his\r\nletters; and having advanced so far, he was not to be hindered now. By\r\nforcing the large blade of his knife under the flap of the bureau, he\r\nburst the weak lock; within was the rosewood work-box just as she had\r\nplaced it in her hurry to keep it from him. There being no time to\r\nspare for getting the letters out of it then, he took it under his arm,\r\nshut the bureau, and made the best of his way out of the house,\r\nlatching the casement behind him, and refixing the pane of glass in its\r\nplace.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Winter found his way back to his mother\xe2\x80\x99s as he had come, and being\r\ndog-tired, crept upstairs to bed, hiding the box till he could destroy\r\nits contents. The next morning early he set about doing this, and\r\ncarried it to the linhay at the back of his mother\xe2\x80\x99s dwelling. Here by\r\nthe hearth he opened the box, and began burning one by one the letters\r\nthat had cost him so much labour to write and shame to think of,\r\nmeaning to return the box to Harriet, after repairing the slight damage\r\nhe had caused it by opening it without a key, with a note\xe2\x80\x94the last she\r\nwould ever receive from him\xe2\x80\x94telling her triumphantly that in refusing\r\nto return what he had asked for she had calculated too surely upon his\r\nsubmission to her whims.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But on removing the last letter from the box he received a shock; for\r\nunderneath it, at the very bottom, lay money\xe2\x80\x94several golden\r\nguineas\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cDoubtless Harriet\xe2\x80\x99s pocket-money,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to himself; though\r\nit was not, but Mrs. Palmley\xe2\x80\x99s. Before he had got over his qualms at\r\nthis discovery he heard footsteps coming through the house-passage to\r\nwhere he was. In haste he pushed the box and what was in it under some\r\nbrushwood which lay in the linhay; but Jack had been already seen. Two\r\nconstables entered the out-house, and seized him as he knelt before the\r\nfireplace, securing the work-box and all it contained at the same\r\nmoment. They had come to apprehend him on a charge of breaking into the\r\ndwelling-house of Mrs. Palmley on the night preceding; and almost\r\nbefore the lad knew what had happened to him they were leading him\r\nalong the lane that connects that end of the village with this\r\nturnpike-road, and along they marched him between \xe2\x80\x99em all the way to\r\nCasterbridge jail.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Jack\xe2\x80\x99s act amounted to night burglary\xe2\x80\x94though he had never thought of\r\nit\xe2\x80\x94and burglary was felony, and a capital offence in those days. His\r\nfigure had been seen by some one against the bright wall as he came\r\naway from Mrs. Palmley\xe2\x80\x99s back window, and the box and money were found\r\nin his possession, while the evidence of the broken bureau-lock and\r\ntinkered window-pane was more than enough for circumstantial detail.\r\nWhether his protestation that he went only for his letters, which he\r\nbelieved to be wrongfully kept from him, would have availed him\r\nanything if supported by other evidence I do not know; but the one\r\nperson who could have borne it out was Harriet, and she acted entirely\r\nunder the sway of her aunt. That aunt was deadly towards Jack Winter.\r\nMrs. Palmley\xe2\x80\x99s time had come. Here was her revenge upon the woman who\r\nhad first won away her lover, and next ruined and deprived her of her\r\nheart\xe2\x80\x99s treasure\xe2\x80\x94her little son. When the assize week drew on, and Jack\r\nhad to stand his trial, Harriet did not appear in the case at all,\r\nwhich was allowed to take its course, Mrs. Palmley testifying to the\r\ngeneral facts of the burglary. Whether Harriet would have come forward\r\nif Jack had appealed to her is not known; possibly she would have done\r\nit for pity\xe2\x80\x99s sake; but Jack was too proud to ask a single favour of a\r\ngirl who had jilted him; and he let her alone. The trial was a short\r\none, and the death sentence was passed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The day o\xe2\x80\x99 young Jack\xe2\x80\x99s execution was a cold dusty Saturday in March.\r\nHe was so boyish and slim that they were obliged in mercy to hang him\r\nin the heaviest fetters kept in the jail, lest his heft should not\r\nbreak his neck, and they weighed so upon him that he could hardly drag\r\nhimself up to the drop. At that time the gover\xe2\x80\x99ment was not strict\r\nabout burying the body of an executed person within the precincts of\r\nthe prison, and at the earnest prayer of his poor mother his body was\r\nallowed to be brought home. All the parish waited at their cottage\r\ndoors in the evening for its arrival: I remember how, as a very little\r\ngirl, I stood by my mother\xe2\x80\x99s side. About eight o\xe2\x80\x99clock, as we hearkened\r\non our door-stones in the cold bright starlight, we could hear the\r\nfaint crackle of a waggon from the direction of the turnpike-road. The\r\nnoise was lost as the waggon dropped into a hollow, then it was plain\r\nagain as it lumbered down the next long incline, and presently it\r\nentered Longpuddle. The coffin was laid in the belfry for the night,\r\nand the next day, Sunday, between the services, we buried him. A\r\nfuneral sermon was preached the same afternoon, the text chosen being,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was the only son of his mother, and she was a widow.\xe2\x80\x9d . . . Yes,\r\nthey were cruel times!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98As for Harriet, she and her lover were married in due time; but by all\r\naccount her life was no jocund one. She and her good-man found that\r\nthey could not live comfortably at Longpuddle, by reason of her\r\nconnection with Jack\xe2\x80\x99s misfortunes, and they settled in a distant town,\r\nand were no more heard of by us; Mrs. Palmley, too, found it advisable\r\nto join \xe2\x80\x99em shortly after. The dark-eyed, gaunt old Mrs. Winter,\r\nremembered by the emigrant gentleman here, was, as you will have\r\nforeseen, the Mrs. Winter of this story; and I can well call to mind\r\nhow lonely she was, how afraid the children were of her, and how she\r\nkept herself as a stranger among us, though she lived so long.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Longpuddle has had her sad experiences as well as her sunny ones,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nsaid Mr. Lackland.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, yes. But I am thankful to say not many like that, though good and\r\nbad have lived among us.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98There was Georgy Crookhill\xe2\x80\x94he was one of the shady sort, as I have\r\nreason to know,\xe2\x80\x99 observed the registrar, with the manner of a man who\r\nwould like to have his say also.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I used to hear what he was as a boy at school.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, as he began so he went on. It never got so far as a hanging\r\nmatter with him, to be sure; but he had some narrow escapes of penal\r\nservitude; and once it was a case of the biter bit.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nINCIDENT IN THE LIFE OF MR. GEORGE CROOKHILL\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98One day,\xe2\x80\x99 the registrar continued, \xe2\x80\x98Georgy was ambling out of\r\nMelchester on a miserable screw, the fair being just over, when he saw\r\nin front of him a fine-looking young farmer riding out of the town in\r\nthe same direction. He was mounted on a good strong handsome animal,\r\nworth fifty guineas if worth a crown. When they were going up Bissett\r\nHill, Georgy made it his business to overtake the young farmer. They\r\npassed the time o\xe2\x80\x99 day to one another; Georgy spoke of the state of the\r\nroads, and jogged alongside the well-mounted stranger in very friendly\r\nconversation. The farmer had not been inclined to say much to Georgy at\r\nfirst, but by degrees he grew quite affable too\xe2\x80\x94as friendly as Georgy\r\nwas toward him. He told Crookhill that he had been doing business at\r\nMelchester fair, and was going on as far as Shottsford-Forum that\r\nnight, so as to reach Casterbridge market the next day. When they came\r\nto Woodyates Inn they stopped to bait their horses, and agreed to drink\r\ntogether; with this they got more friendly than ever, and on they went\r\nagain. Before they had nearly reached Shottsford it came on to rain,\r\nand as they were now passing through the village of Trantridge, and it\r\nwas quite dark, Georgy persuaded the young farmer to go no further that\r\nnight; the rain would most likely give them a chill. For his part he\r\nhad heard that the little inn here was comfortable, and he meant to\r\nstay. At last the young farmer agreed to put up there also; and they\r\ndismounted, and entered, and had a good supper together, and talked\r\nover their affairs like men who had known and proved each other a long\r\ntime. When it was the hour for retiring they went upstairs to a\r\ndouble-bedded room which Georgy Crookhill had asked the landlord to let\r\nthem share, so sociable were they.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Before they fell asleep they talked across the room about one thing\r\nand another, running from this to that till the conversation turned\r\nupon disguises, and changing clothes for particular ends. The farmer\r\ntold Georgy that he had often heard tales of people doing it; but\r\nCrookhill professed to be very ignorant of all such tricks; and soon\r\nthe young farmer sank into slumber.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Early in the morning, while the tall young farmer was still asleep (I\r\ntell the story as \xe2\x80\x99twas told me), honest Georgy crept out of his bed by\r\nstealth, and dressed himself in the farmer\xe2\x80\x99s clothes, in the pockets of\r\nthe said clothes being the farmer\xe2\x80\x99s money. Now though Georgy\r\nparticularly wanted the farmer\xe2\x80\x99s nice clothes and nice horse, owing to\r\na little transaction at the fair which made it desirable that he should\r\nnot be too easily recognized, his desires had their bounds: he did not\r\nwish to take his young friend\xe2\x80\x99s money, at any rate more of it than was\r\nnecessary for paying his bill. This he abstracted, and leaving the\r\nfarmer\xe2\x80\x99s purse containing the rest on the bedroom table, went\r\ndownstairs. The inn folks had not particularly noticed the faces of\r\ntheir customers, and the one or two who were up at this hour had no\r\nthought but that Georgy was the farmer; so when he had paid the bill\r\nvery liberally, and said he must be off, no objection was made to his\r\ngetting the farmer\xe2\x80\x99s horse saddled for himself; and he rode away upon\r\nit as if it were his own.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98About half an hour after the young farmer awoke, and looking across\r\nthe room saw that his friend Georgy had gone away in clothes which\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t belong to him, and had kindly left for himself the seedy ones\r\nworn by Georgy. At this he sat up in a deep thought for some time,\r\ninstead of hastening to give an alarm. \xe2\x80\x9cThe money, the money is gone,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe said to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cand that\xe2\x80\x99s bad. But so are the clothes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He then looked upon the table and saw that the money, or most of it,\r\nhad been left behind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cHa, ha, ha!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried, and began to dance about the room. \xe2\x80\x9cHa, ha,\r\nha!\xe2\x80\x9d he said again, and made beautiful smiles to himself in the shaving\r\nglass and in the brass candlestick; and then swung about his arms for\r\nall the world as if he were going through the sword exercise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98When he had dressed himself in Georgy\xe2\x80\x99s clothes and gone downstairs,\r\nhe did not seem to mind at all that they took him for the other; and\r\neven when he saw that he had been left a bad horse for a good one, he\r\nwas not inclined to cry out. They told him his friend had paid the\r\nbill, at which he seemed much pleased, and without waiting for\r\nbreakfast he mounted Georgy\xe2\x80\x99s horse and rode away likewise, choosing\r\nthe nearest by-lane in preference to the high-road, without knowing\r\nthat Georgy had chosen that by-lane also.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He had not trotted more than two miles in the personal character of\r\nGeorgy Crookhill when, suddenly rounding a bend that the lane made\r\nthereabout, he came upon a man struggling in the hands of two village\r\nconstables. It was his friend Georgy, the borrower of his clothes and\r\nhorse. But so far was the young farmer from showing any alacrity in\r\nrushing forward to claim his property that he would have turned the\r\npoor beast he rode into the wood adjoining, if he had not been already\r\nperceived.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cHelp, help, help!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the constables. \xe2\x80\x9cAssistance in the name of\r\nthe Crown!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The young farmer could do nothing but ride forward. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\nmatter?\xe2\x80\x9d he inquired, as coolly as he could.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cA deserter\xe2\x80\x94a deserter!\xe2\x80\x9d said they. \xe2\x80\x9cOne who\xe2\x80\x99s to be tried by\r\ncourt-martial and shot without parley. He deserted from the Dragoons at\r\nCheltenham some days ago, and was tracked; but the search-party can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nfind him anywhere, and we told \xe2\x80\x99em if we met him we\xe2\x80\x99d hand him on to\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em forthwith. The day after he left the barracks the rascal met a\r\nrespectable farmer and made him drunk at an inn, and told him what a\r\nfine soldier he would make, and coaxed him to change clothes, to see\r\nhow well a military uniform would become him. This the simple farmer\r\ndid; when our deserter said that for a joke he would leave the room and\r\ngo to the landlady, to see if she would know him in that dress. He\r\nnever came back, and Farmer Jollice found himself in soldier\xe2\x80\x99s clothes,\r\nthe money in his pockets gone, and, when he got to the stable, his\r\nhorse gone too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cA scoundrel!\xe2\x80\x9d says the young man in Georgy\xe2\x80\x99s clothes. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd is this\r\nthe wretched caitiff?\xe2\x80\x9d (pointing to Georgy).\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no!\xe2\x80\x9d cries Georgy, as innocent as a babe of this matter of the\r\nsoldier\xe2\x80\x99s desertion. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s the man! He was wearing Farmer Jollice\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsuit o\xe2\x80\x99 clothes, and he slept in the same room wi\xe2\x80\x99 me, and brought up\r\nthe subject of changing clothes, which put it into my head to dress\r\nmyself in his suit before he was awake. He\xe2\x80\x99s got on mine!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cD\xe2\x80\x99ye hear the villain?\xe2\x80\x9d groans the tall young man to the constables.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrying to get out of his crime by charging the first innocent man with\r\nit that he sees! No, master soldier\xe2\x80\x94that won\xe2\x80\x99t do!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no! That won\xe2\x80\x99t do!\xe2\x80\x9d the constables chimed in. \xe2\x80\x9cTo have the\r\nimpudence to say such as that, when we caught him in the act almost!\r\nBut, thank God, we\xe2\x80\x99ve got the handcuffs on him at last.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cWe have, thank God,\xe2\x80\x9d said the tall young man. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I must move on.\r\nGood luck to ye with your prisoner!\xe2\x80\x9d And off he went, as fast as his\r\npoor jade would carry him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The constables then, with Georgy handcuffed between \xe2\x80\x99em, and leading\r\nthe horse, marched off in the other direction, toward the village where\r\nthey had been accosted by the escort of soldiers sent to bring the\r\ndeserter back, Georgy groaning: \xe2\x80\x9cI shall be shot, I shall be shot!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nThey had not gone more than a mile before they met them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cHoi, there!\xe2\x80\x9d says the head constable.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cHoi, yerself!\xe2\x80\x9d says the corporal in charge.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve got your man,\xe2\x80\x9d says the constable.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cWhere?\xe2\x80\x9d says the corporal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cHere, between us,\xe2\x80\x9d said the constable. \xe2\x80\x9cOnly you don\xe2\x80\x99t recognize him\r\nout o\xe2\x80\x99 uniform.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The corporal looked at Georgy hard enough; then shook his head and\r\nsaid he was not the absconder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cBut the absconder changed clothes with Farmer Jollice, and took his\r\nhorse; and this man has \xe2\x80\x99em, d\xe2\x80\x99ye see!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis not our man,\xe2\x80\x9d said the soldiers. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s a tall young fellow with\r\na mole on his right cheek, and a military bearing, which this man\r\ndecidedly has not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cI told the two officers of justice that \xe2\x80\x99twas the other!\xe2\x80\x9d pleaded\r\nGeorgy. \xe2\x80\x9cBut they wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t believe me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And so it became clear that the missing dragoon was the tall young\r\nfarmer, and not Georgy Crookhill\xe2\x80\x94a fact which Farmer Jollice himself\r\ncorroborated when he arrived on the scene. As Georgy had only robbed\r\nthe robber, his sentence was comparatively light. The deserter from the\r\nDragoons was never traced: his double shift of clothing having been of\r\nthe greatest advantage to him in getting off; though he left Georgy\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhorse behind him a few miles ahead, having found the poor creature more\r\nhindrance than aid.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\nThe man from abroad seemed to be less interested in the questionable\r\ncharacters of Longpuddle and their strange adventures than in the\r\nordinary inhabitants and the ordinary events, though his local\r\nfellow-travellers preferred the former as subjects of discussion. He\r\nnow for the first time asked concerning young persons of the opposite\r\nsex\xe2\x80\x94or rather those who had been young when he left his native land.\r\nHis informants, adhering to their own opinion that the remarkable was\r\nbetter worth telling than the ordinary, would not allow him to dwell\r\nupon the simple chronicles of those who had merely come and gone. They\r\nasked him if he remembered Netty Sargent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Netty Sargent\xe2\x80\x94I do, just remember her. She was a young woman living\r\nwith her uncle when I left, if my childish recollection may be\r\ntrusted.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That was the maid. She was a oneyer, if you like, sir. Not any harm in\r\nher, you know, but up to everything. You ought to hear how she got the\r\ncopyhold of her house extended. Oughtn\xe2\x80\x99t he, Mr. Day?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He ought,\xe2\x80\x99 replied the world-ignored old painter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Tell him, Mr. Day. Nobody can do it better than you, and you know the\r\nlegal part better than some of us.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nDay apologized, and began:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nNETTY SARGENT\xe2\x80\x99S COPYHOLD\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98She continued to live with her uncle, in the lonely house by the\r\ncopse, just as at the time you knew her; a tall spry young woman. Ah,\r\nhow well one can remember her black hair and dancing eyes at that time,\r\nand her sly way of screwing up her mouth when she meant to tease ye!\r\nWell, she was hardly out of short frocks before the chaps were after\r\nher, and by long and by late she was courted by a young man whom\r\nperhaps you did not know\xe2\x80\x94Jasper Cliff was his name\xe2\x80\x94and, though she\r\nmight have had many a better fellow, he so greatly took her fancy that\r\n\xe2\x80\x99twas Jasper or nobody for her. He was a selfish customer, always\r\nthinking less of what he was going to do than of what he was going to\r\ngain by his doings. Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s eyes might have been fixed upon Netty, but\r\nhis mind was upon her uncle\xe2\x80\x99s house; though he was fond of her in his\r\nway\xe2\x80\x94I admit that.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98This house, built by her great-great-grandfather, with its garden and\r\nlittle field, was copyhold\xe2\x80\x94granted upon lives in the old way, and had\r\nbeen so granted for generations. Her uncle\xe2\x80\x99s was the last life upon the\r\nproperty; so that at his death, if there was no admittance of new\r\nlives, it would all fall into the hands of the lord of the manor. But\r\n\xe2\x80\x99twas easy to admit\xe2\x80\x94a slight \xe2\x80\x9cfine,\xe2\x80\x9d as \xe2\x80\x99twas called, of a few pounds,\r\nwas enough to entitle him to a new deed o\xe2\x80\x99 grant by the custom of the\r\nmanor; and the lord could not hinder it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Now there could be no better provision for his niece and only relative\r\nthan a sure house over her head, and Netty\xe2\x80\x99s uncle should have seen to\r\nthe renewal in time, owing to the peculiar custom of forfeiture by the\r\ndropping of the last life before the new fine was paid; for the Squire\r\nwas very anxious to get hold of the house and land; and every Sunday\r\nwhen the old man came into the church and passed the Squire\xe2\x80\x99s pew, the\r\nSquire would say, \xe2\x80\x9cA little weaker in his knees, a little crookeder in\r\nhis back\xe2\x80\x94and the readmittance not applied for: ha! ha! I shall be able\r\nto make a complete clearing of that corner of the manor some day!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x99Twas extraordinary, now we look back upon it, that old Sargent should\r\nhave been so dilatory; yet some people are like it; and he put off\r\ncalling at the Squire\xe2\x80\x99s agent\xe2\x80\x99s office with the fine week after week,\r\nsaying to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cI shall have more time next market-day than I have\r\nnow.\xe2\x80\x9d One unfortunate hindrance was that he didn\xe2\x80\x99t very well like\r\nJasper Cliff; and as Jasper kept urging Netty, and Netty on that\r\naccount kept urging her uncle, the old man was inclined to postpone the\r\nre-liveing as long as he could, to spite the selfish young lover. At\r\nlast old Mr. Sargent fell ill, and then Jasper could bear it no longer:\r\nhe produced the fine-money himself, and handed it to Netty, and spoke\r\nto her plainly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cYou and your uncle ought to know better. You should press him more.\r\nThere\xe2\x80\x99s the money. If you let the house and ground slip between ye, I\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t marry; hang me if I will! For folks won\xe2\x80\x99t deserve a husband that\r\ncan do such things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The worried girl took the money and went home, and told her uncle that\r\nit was no house no husband for her. Old Mr. Sargent pooh-poohed the\r\nmoney, for the amount was not worth consideration, but he did now\r\nbestir himself; for he saw she was bent upon marrying Jasper, and he\r\ndid not wish to make her unhappy, since she was so determined. It was\r\nmuch to the Squire\xe2\x80\x99s annoyance that he found Sargent had moved in the\r\nmatter at last; but he could not gainsay it, and the documents were\r\nprepared (for on this manor the copy-holders had writings with their\r\nholdings, though on some manors they had none). Old Sargent being now\r\ntoo feeble to go to the agent\xe2\x80\x99s house, the deed was to be brought to\r\nhis house signed, and handed over as a receipt for the money; the\r\ncounterpart to be signed by Sargent, and sent back to the Squire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The agent had promised to call on old Sargent for this purpose at five\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock, and Netty put the money into her desk to have it close at\r\nhand. While doing this she heard a slight cry from her uncle, and\r\nturning round, saw that he had fallen forward in his chair. She went\r\nand lifted him, but he was unconscious; and unconscious he remained.\r\nNeither medicine nor stimulants would bring him to himself. She had\r\nbeen told that he might possibly go off in that way, and it seemed as\r\nif the end had come. Before she had started for a doctor his face and\r\nextremities grew quite cold and white, and she saw that help would be\r\nuseless. He was stone-dead.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Netty\xe2\x80\x99s situation rose upon her distracted mind in all its\r\nseriousness. The house, garden, and field were lost\xe2\x80\x94by a few hours\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nwith them a home for herself and her lover. She would not think so\r\nmeanly of Jasper as to suppose that he would adhere to the resolution\r\ndeclared in a moment of impatience; but she trembled, nevertheless. Why\r\ncould not her uncle have lived a couple of hours longer, since he had\r\nlived so long? It was now past three o\xe2\x80\x99clock; at five the agent was to\r\ncall, and, if all had gone well, by ten minutes past five the house and\r\nholding would have been securely hers for her own and Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s lives,\r\nthese being two of the three proposed to be added by paying the fine.\r\nHow that wretched old Squire would rejoice at getting the little\r\ntenancy into his hands! He did not really require it, but\r\nconstitutionally hated these tiny copyholds and leaseholds and\r\nfreeholds, which made islands of independence in the fair, smooth ocean\r\nof his estates.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then an idea struck into the head of Netty how to accomplish her\r\nobject in spite of her uncle\xe2\x80\x99s negligence. It was a dull December\r\nafternoon: and the first step in her scheme\xe2\x80\x94so the story goes, and I\r\nsee no reason to doubt it\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x99Tis true as the light,\xe2\x80\x99 affirmed Christopher Twink. \xe2\x80\x98I was just\r\npassing by.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The first step in her scheme was to fasten the outer door, to make\r\nsure of not being interrupted. Then she set to work by placing her\r\nuncle\xe2\x80\x99s small, heavy oak table before the fire; then she went to her\r\nuncle\xe2\x80\x99s corpse, sitting in the chair as he had died\xe2\x80\x94a stuffed\r\narm-chair, on casters, and rather high in the seat, so it was told\r\nme\xe2\x80\x94and wheeled the chair, uncle and all, to the table, placing him with\r\nhis back toward the window, in the attitude of bending over the said\r\noak table, which I knew as a boy as well as I know any piece of\r\nfurniture in my own house. On the table she laid the large family Bible\r\nopen before him, and placed his forefinger on the page; and then she\r\nopened his eyelids a bit, and put on him his spectacles, so that from\r\nbehind he appeared for all the world as if he were reading the\r\nScriptures. Then she unfastened the door and sat down, and when it grew\r\ndark she lit a candle, and put it on the table beside her uncle\xe2\x80\x99s book.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Folk may well guess how the time passed with her till the agent came,\r\nand how, when his knock sounded upon the door, she nearly started out\r\nof her skin\xe2\x80\x94at least that\xe2\x80\x99s as it was told me. Netty promptly went to\r\nthe door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d she says, under her breath; \xe2\x80\x9cmy uncle is not so\r\nwell to-night, and I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid he can\xe2\x80\x99t see you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cH\xe2\x80\x99m!\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s a pretty tale,\xe2\x80\x9d says the steward. \xe2\x80\x9cSo I\xe2\x80\x99ve come all this\r\nway about this trumpery little job for nothing!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cO no, sir\xe2\x80\x94I hope not,\xe2\x80\x9d says Netty. \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose the business of\r\ngranting the new deed can be done just the same?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cDone? Certainly not. He must pay the renewal money, and sign the\r\nparchment in my presence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98She looked dubious. \xe2\x80\x9cUncle is so dreadful nervous about law business,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsays she, \xe2\x80\x9cthat, as you know, he\xe2\x80\x99s put it off and put it off for years;\r\nand now to-day really I\xe2\x80\x99ve feared it would verily drive him out of his\r\nmind. His poor three teeth quite chattered when I said to him that you\r\nwould be here soon with the parchment writing. He always was afraid of\r\nagents, and folks that come for rent, and such-like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cPoor old fellow\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m sorry for him. Well, the thing can\xe2\x80\x99t be done\r\nunless I see him and witness his signature.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cSuppose, sir, that you see him sign, and he don\xe2\x80\x99t see you looking at\r\nhim? I\xe2\x80\x99d soothe his nerves by saying you weren\xe2\x80\x99t strict about the form\r\nof witnessing, and didn\xe2\x80\x99t wish to come in. So that it was done in your\r\nbare presence it would be sufficient, would it not? As he\xe2\x80\x99s such an\r\nold, shrinking, shivering man, it would be a great considerateness on\r\nyour part if that would do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cIn my bare presence would do, of course\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s all I come for. But\r\nhow can I be a witness without his seeing me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, in this way, sir; if you\xe2\x80\x99ll oblige me by just stepping here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nShe conducted him a few yards to the left, till they were opposite the\r\nparlour window. The blind had been left up purposely, and the\r\ncandle-light shone out upon the garden bushes. Within the agent could\r\nsee, at the other end of the room, the back and side of the old man\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhead, and his shoulders and arm, sitting with the book and candle\r\nbefore him, and his spectacles on his nose, as she had placed him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s reading his Bible, as you see, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d she says, quite in her\r\nmeekest way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cYes. I thought he was a careless sort of man in matters of religion?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cHe always was fond of his Bible,\xe2\x80\x9d Netty assured him. \xe2\x80\x9cThough I think\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99s nodding over it just at this moment However, that\xe2\x80\x99s natural in an\r\nold man, and unwell. Now you could stand here and see him sign,\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you, sir, as he\xe2\x80\x99s such an invalid?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cVery well,\xe2\x80\x9d said the agent, lighting a cigar. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have ready by you\r\nthe merely nominal sum you\xe2\x80\x99ll have to pay for the admittance, of\r\ncourse?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Netty. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll bring it out.\xe2\x80\x9d She fetched the cash, wrapped\r\nin paper, and handed it to him, and when he had counted it the steward\r\ntook from his breast pocket the precious parchments and gave one to her\r\nto be signed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cUncle\xe2\x80\x99s hand is a little paralyzed,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd what with his\r\nbeing half asleep, too, really I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what sort of a signature\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99ll be able to make.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cDoesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter, so that he signs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cMight I hold his hand?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cAy, hold his hand, my young woman\xe2\x80\x94that will be near enough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Netty re-entered the house, and the agent continued smoking outside\r\nthe window. Now came the ticklish part of Netty\xe2\x80\x99s performance. The\r\nsteward saw her put the inkhorn\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9chorn,\xe2\x80\x9d says I in my old-fashioned\r\nway\xe2\x80\x94the inkstand, before her uncle, and touch his elbow as to arouse\r\nhim, and speak to him, and spread out the deed; when she had pointed to\r\nshow him where to sign she dipped the pen and put it into his hand. To\r\nhold his hand she artfully stepped behind him, so that the agent could\r\nonly see a little bit of his head, and the hand she held; but he saw\r\nthe old man\xe2\x80\x99s hand trace his name on the document. As soon as \xe2\x80\x99twas\r\ndone she came out to the steward with the parchment in her hand, and\r\nthe steward signed as witness by the light from the parlour window.\r\nThen he gave her the deed signed by the Squire, and left; and next\r\nmorning Netty told the neighbours that her uncle was dead in his bed.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98She must have undressed him and put him there.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98She must. Oh, that girl had a nerve, I can tell ye! Well, to cut a\r\nlong story short, that\xe2\x80\x99s how she got back the house and field that\r\nwere, strictly speaking, gone from her; and by getting them, got her a\r\nhusband.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Every virtue has its reward, they say. Netty had hers for her\r\ningenious contrivance to gain Jasper. Two years after they were married\r\nhe took to beating her\xe2\x80\x94not hard, you know; just a smack or two, enough\r\nto set her in a temper, and let out to the neighbours what she had done\r\nto win him, and how she repented of her pains. When the old Squire was\r\ndead, and his son came into the property, this confession of hers began\r\nto be whispered about. But Netty was a pretty young woman, and the\r\nSquire\xe2\x80\x99s son was a pretty young man at that time, and wider-minded than\r\nhis father, having no objection to little holdings; and he never took\r\nany proceedings against her.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThere was now a lull in the discourse, and soon the van descended the\r\nhill leading into the long straggling village. When the houses were\r\nreached the passengers dropped off one by one, each at his or her own\r\ndoor. Arrived at the inn, the returned emigrant secured a bed, and\r\nhaving eaten a light meal, sallied forth upon the scene he had known so\r\nwell in his early days. Though flooded with the light of the rising\r\nmoon, none of the objects wore the attractiveness in this their real\r\npresentation that had ever accompanied their images in the field of his\r\nimagination when he was more than two thousand miles removed from them.\r\nThe peculiar charm attaching to an old village in an old country, as\r\nseen by the eyes of an absolute foreigner, was lowered in his case by\r\nmagnified expectations from infantine memories. He walked on, looking\r\nat this chimney and that old wall, till he came to the churchyard,\r\nwhich he entered.\r\n\r\nThe head-stones, whitened by the moon, were easily decipherable; and\r\nnow for the first time Lackland began to feel himself amid the village\r\ncommunity that he had left behind him five-and-thirty years before.\r\nHere, besides the Sallets, the Darths, the Pawles, the Privetts, the\r\nSargents, and others of whom he had just heard, were names he\r\nremembered even better than those: the Jickses, and the Crosses, and\r\nthe Knights, and the Olds. Doubtless representatives of these families,\r\nor some of them, were yet among the living; but to him they would all\r\nbe as strangers. Far from finding his heart ready-supplied with roots\r\nand tendrils here, he perceived that in returning to this spot it would\r\nbe incumbent upon him to re-establish himself from the beginning,\r\nprecisely as though he had never known the place, nor it him. Time had\r\nnot condescended to wait his pleasure, nor local life his greeting.\r\n\r\nThe figure of Mr. Lackland was seen at the inn, and in the village\r\nstreet, and in the fields and lanes about Upper Longpuddle, for a few\r\ndays after his arrival, and then, ghost-like, it silently disappeared.\r\nHe had told some of the villagers that his immediate purpose in coming\r\nhad been fulfilled by a sight of the place, and by conversation with\r\nits inhabitants: but that his ulterior purpose\xe2\x80\x94of coming to spend his\r\nlatter days among them\xe2\x80\x94would probably never be carried out. It is now a\r\ndozen or fifteen years since his visit was paid, and his face has not\r\nagain been seen.\r\n\r\n_March_ 1891.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n*** END OF THE PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK LIFE\xe2\x80\x99S LITTLE IRONIES ***\r\n\r\nUpdated editions will replace the previous one--the old editions will\r\nbe renamed.\r\n\r\nCreating the works from print editions not protected by U.S. copyright\r\nlaw means that no one owns a United States copyright in these works,\r\nso the Foundation (and you!) can copy and distribute it in the\r\nUnited States without permission and without paying copyright\r\nroyalties.
